ARCHBISHOP
CORRIGAN TO
THE POPE
So-Called "Americanism" Is
Denounced by the
Prelate.
..-.../'â 'â ..-.
CALLS IT A MONSTER
Pleased His Holiness Has Rooted
Out "This Cockle From the
Field of Wheat."
Social Dispatch to The Call.
XEW YORK, April 30.â A Washing
ton special to the Herald says: Arch
bishop Corrigan's letter to the Pop*, on.
so-called "Americanism" in the Roman
Catholic church has reached Washing
ton, and its translation into English
has caused a sensation in Catholic
circles.
Cardinal Gibbons, Archbishops Ire
land and Keane and even conservative
Archbishop Ketzer of Milwaukee and
Archbishop- Horstmann of Cleveland,
solemnly protested to the Pope that
the errors which ho had designated as
"Americanism" had no place in the
American Roman Catholic church.
The Archbishop of New York admits
the charges against the American Ro
man Catholic, but stands alone am >ng
the American hierarchy.
In the course of his letter. Archbish
op Corrigan calls "Americanism" "a
monster which, in order to acquire the
rights of citizenship among us, has as
sumed that fair name," and' he adds
later. "Your Holiness roots out on its
very appearance this cockle from the
field of wheat." The letter is as fol
lows:
Most Holy Father: We cannot express
in words the feelings of admiration, of
joy and gratitude with which our heart
has been penetrated toward your Holi
ness in reading the masterly and admir
able letter which you have deigned to
adiiress to us on what for some time past
has been designated under the name of
"Americanism."
It is with greatest wisdom that your
Holiness has united into one group many
and fallacious errors which would like
to pass for good Catholic teaching under
the specious title of "Americanism." but
at the same time with what great pru
dence, discretion and kindness as well as
with what force and clearness has ypur
Holiness fulfilled the mission of a sup: .
and infallible teacher.';
-In truth the last document of your
Holiness in wisdom is in no way inferor
to so many others which in the course of
your glorious pontificate have excited the
universal admiration of peoples.
For us. whom the Holy Ghost has
placed as Bishops to rule the church of
God tinder the infallible guidance of your
Holiness, we hasten to offer ami to make
known to you our sentiments of admir
ing and unqualified adhesion.
We receive and accept for ourselves,
our clergy and. for the communities and
congregations which with us work for
the salvation of souls, as well as for ail
"Testem benevolentae." We accept it
and make it our own, word for word,
sentence for sentence, in the same iden
tical sense in which your Holiness, fol
lowing the tradition and wisdom of all
Christian antiquity. intends it, and
wishes that it # should be understood by
us. *
In its regard we shall never make, nor
shall we ever permit that others depend
ing from us, either directly or indirectly,
should make any reservation or tergiv
ersation. Your Holiness has spoken. The
question is therefore ended. This thought
has given us great satisfaction.
We can say also that the monster,
which in order to obtain a lasting abode,
to acquire rights of citizenship among
us, assumed to itself the fair name of
"Americanism," has on its first appear
ance been struck down dead. But it is to
you that the glory of this happy result is
due.
If your Holiness had not opportunely
come to our aid with your admirable let
ter, how numerous might have been those
who through ignorance, rather than
malice, would have been brought into
the trap. Bishops and clergy would
have had hard work to keep their people
out of error. It would have taken root
little by little, always increasing, and
soon we should have been pointed at with
the accusation of not being Americans.
Meanwhile, this false "Americanism,"
understood like other similar titles,
which to the great delight of souls lasted
I f&r res among other nations, would have
tranquilly taken possession ln our midst.
; piling high up its conquests in enormous
proportions of time and place. it is there
fore that we rejoice greatly, that by rea
son of your infallible teaching we have
not to transmit to our successors the un
grateful task of fighting an enemy which
would not have died.
And now, with our heads high, we can
repeat that we are Americans as truly
as any one, whoever he may be. Yes, we,
are, and we glory in it. \\ .â  glory In it
because our nation Is great in its Insti
tutions and in its undertakings; great In
Its development and in its activity: but
in the matter of religion and the doctrine
of discipline, of moralty, of Christian
perfection, .we glory in following Implic
itly the Holy See.
For these reasons we are and shall ever
be most grateful to your Holiness, who,
by your imperishable letter, "Testem
benevolentae," has said to us and to all
Catholics of America, "Signal blessing."
Yes, by testimony of kindness your Holi
ness roots out on its very appearance the
cockle from the field of -wheat.
May the all-powerful Lord preserve the
health of your Holiness, for many more
years, so that your Holiness may. with
your own eyes, see ripen to perfectness
the fruit of your, apostolic vigilance. .
Prostrate at the feet of your Holiness,
and kissing them with profound respect,
we implore for us, for our clergy and for
our flock apostolic benediction.
For Bishops of â¢ ecclesiastical province,
your province. ,*..- s * . .
MICHAEL AUGUSTINE.
Archbishop of New York.
New York. March 19. 1899/ * ""

SPOKANE, Wash., April 30.â A1l is
quiet in the Coeur d'Alenes to
night, although the decent people
of Wardner, Wallace, Burke, Gem
and other towns in the district
have no feeling of security, and many
of them are coming into Spokane.
Most of the rioting miners who went
from Wallace to participate in the de
struction of the Bunker Hill and Sulli
van property have returned to their
jobs in the union mines. â  - >
Between fifty and sixty of the non
union men who escaped from the Bun
ker Hill and Sullivan have returned to
that property after a night in the hills
j and are so far unmolested.
Through the Coeur d'Alene towns' a
reaction has set in and the destruction
of so much property, throwing 1000
, men out of employment and thereby
paralyzing all business in Wardner, is
now being condemned.
Coroner France of Shoshone County
j has created a sensation by declaring
that he will conduct a searching in
j quest into the death of Jack Smith, the
; rioting miner killed in the fusillade
! when two bands of rioters fired on each
other by mistake^
"No straw verdict," said he, "will be
accepted." This is recognized as some
thing of an innovation. fyf
"I shall summon every man in the ,
county if necessary in order to ascer
tain the cause of his death," said Coro
ner France decisively to-night. "No
straw .-verdict will 'be -.received. I shall
continue the hearing as long as may
be necessary."
Smith's body is 'now lying in 'the
Miners' Union Hall at Burke,. where it
was taken by the rioters on their re
turn there yesterday' afternoon.. At
first -some difficulty was looked for in
holding the inquest.. The 'miners sent
word down to Dr. France, the Coroner,
I that he would be expected to come up
there in order to examine into the'
cause of death. They added that in
case he did not see .fit to do so, one of
the local magistrates would be called
I upon to conduct, the examination. Dr.
France /promptly telephoned to. Paul
Cochrane, the secretary of the Burke
union, and held him responsible for the
safe keeping of the: remains until the
Coroner himself could conduct the
hearing. ' .ff ffy 'â¢:*..";. '"f'-i-'f;ff.-l* s "iff.

back that the body would-be' held. there
subject to the , orders of , Dr. jj France.

! lace undertakers to bring ; the ' body

be. brought back here -to-morrow..

GO ON THE
WARPATH
Attack a Party of Whites
at a Camp Near Haines
Mission.
SOLDIERS SENT OUT
The Outbreak Incited by Men Op
posed to the Construction of
* a New Trail.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SKAGUAY, Alaska, April 24 (via
Seattle, April 30).â Chilkat Indians
are on the warpath. Last Sunday
twenty bucks armed with rifles gath
ered from their several villages along
the Chilkat River and marched to
Rocky Point, eight miles from Haines
Mission, where eight white men were
blasting rock for a trail to Klukwan.
a distance of twenty-five miles. The
Indians drove the whites away.
Word was immediately sent to the
United States Marshal 'and .Commis
sioner at Skaguay and to Captain
Yeatman, in command of the troops at
Dyea. Sixteen soldiers under command
of a sergeant were dispatched to
Haines Mission.
It is understood men who were op-
I posed to the construction of the new
trail have incited the Indians to make
trouble. fyfff'f.
The Dominion Telegraph Company is
making rapid progress with its line
from Lake Bennett to Dawson. The
line has been located fifteen miles down
Bake Bennett, and poles have been dis
tributed almost to White Horse Rapids.

BRITON CRIES "LIAR"
Vast Assemblage of People to Pro
test Against the Policy of
Holding the Philippines.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CHICAGO, April 30.â Central Music
Hall was this after filled
with an audience gathered to pro
test against the course of the ad
ministration with reference to the
Philippines. Once or twice the proceed
ings were interrupted by protests of
those who differed from the sentiments
of the speakers.
Professor J. Lawrence Laughlin. of
the University of Chicago, in speaking
of the national policy toward the Fili
pinos, said:
"There the flag is the emblem of
tyranny and butchery."
Cries of "treason" came from the gal
lery, but the cries were quickly
drowned out by approving cheers.
When Bishop J. L. Spalding of Pe
oria declared that "England had never
been a friend of this country," an Eng
lishman near the platform cried.
"That's a lie." Without noticing the
interruption' the Bishop continued his
address.
Dr. Henry Wade Rogers, Professor
of the Northwestern University, acted
as chairman. Besides him, the speak
ers were Professor Laughlin, Bishop
Spalding, J. Lloyd Jones, Edwin Bur
rill Smith, Sigmund Zeister and Miss
Jane Adams.
The following resolutions were
adopted:

We have never looked upon ourselves as
predestined to subdue the earth, to com
pel other nations with sword and fire to
accept our rule.-; we have always believed
in human rights. In freedom and oppor
tunity, in education and religion and we
have Invited all men to me and enjoy
these blessings in this half a world
which God has given us, but we have
never dreamed that they were articles to
be exported and thrust down unwilling
throats at the point of th. bayonet. We
have sympathized with all oppressed peo
piesâ Ireland. Greece, Armenia and
Cuba. To emancipate the slave we
gladly sacrificed the lives of hundreds of
thousands of our soldiers. And now the
American soldier, who never shoulders a
gun except in a righteous cause, is sent
10.000 miles across the ocean to shoot men
whose real crime is that they wish to be
free, wish to govern themselves. To
say that they are unfit for freedom is to
put forth the plea of the tyrant in all
ages and everywhere. The enemies of
liberty have never lacked for pretexts to
Justify their wrong, but. In truth, at the
root of all wars of conquest there lies lust
for blood or for gold.
If the inhabitants of the Philippines
came gladly to throw themselves into our
arms we should refuse to do more than
counsel, guide and protect them, until
they form themselves into a stable and
Independent government. What, then, is
to be thought of those who seem resolved
either to rule or exterminate them, believ
ing, probably, that the only good Filipino
is a dead Filipino?
The argument that our policy has from
the beginning been one of expansion, has
no application In the present matter. In
purchasing Louisiana and New Mexico
and I'pper California, including Nevada
and Utah and most of Arizona and a
part of Colorado, it may be said that we
bought land and not human beings land
that was part of our inheritance. But,
now. following the lead of our great capi
talists and trust lords, we buy at one
stroke 10,000,000 human beings, who live in
another hemisphere, who differ from us In
every way. who dwell in a climate that
is fatal to the white man, who can be of
no advantage whatever to us. but who,
if we persist ia holding them, will involve
us in the most serious difficulties and
dangers.
A war of conquest is contrary
to our fundamental principles of gov
ernment; it is opposeu to all our tradi
tions. The thought of ruling over subject
peoples Is repugnant to our deepest and
noblest sentiment. We have room for
three or four hundred mi. ..ons of human
beings. If more are required, and we are
true to ourselves, British America will
come to us wn.iout there being need of
firing a gun. We have money enough
already and our wealth is rapio -- Increas
ing. What we have to learn is how to
live how to distribute our money, how
to take it from its mastery over us and
make It our servant. â¢
Our capital is fast becoming the most
inhuman, the most iniquitous tyrant the
world has ever known. Its tyranny is a
blight and a curse to those who exercise
It as well as to the multiutde who are its
victims. Commercial and manufacturing
competition is becoming a fierce struggle
for existence. Whereas the tendency of
true civilization and religion is to con
vert the struggle for life Into co-opera
tion for life, Into work of all for all, that
all may have those Inner goods which
make men wis., holy, beautiful and
strong; whereas, this Is the tendency of
right civilization, our greed, our super
stitious belief in money as the only true
god and savior of man. hurries us on with
increasing speed into all the venalties,
dishonesties and corruptions. Into all the
tricks and trusts, by which the people
are disheartened and impoverish
If lt is our destiny to become an empire,
It is not our destiny to endure as a re
public Empire and imperialism are asso
ciation with kingly and arbitrary rule.
with militarism nnd conquest. Was not
the Roman empire built upon the re
public?
But, we are told that imperialism has
proved a blessing to Great Britain. In
this matter there is no parity between
England and the United States. If to
day England could be blockaded for six
months, she would starve. She is
compelled, therefore, to have a navy
as strong almost as that of all the other
nations: and this has led her to make
conquest after conquest until her empire
encircles the earth. But these widely
scattered dominions, though possibly
necessary for her existence as a first-class
power, are for her a cause of weakness.
Let her colonies but become dissatisfied
and they will fall from her as easily as
the ripe" fruit falls from the bough. She
governs them wisely, because only in this
wav can she govern them at all. â¢
It is hardly possible for an American
to speak of England and not feel grateful
thoughts and kindly sentiments stir with
in his breast. To her largely we owe our
liberties: to her our language; to her
Shakespeare. Milton and Wadsworth: to
her Bacon and Ruskin and Newman. Nev
ertheless the more we hold aloof from
England the better shall it be for Amer
ica. She has not an ally In the world
and there is probably not a nation in the
world which would trust her as an ally.
She has never loved us from the days in
which she oppressed the colonies to
the dark days when, by aiding the Con
federacy, she sought to make, the dis
ruption of the Union permanent. She does
not love us now. We are the most dread
ed rival she has. because we threaten her
supremacy in what is nearest and dear
est to herâ her finances. She is -confront
ed by difficulties and dangers of various
kinds from which we ire free.
An alliance with her would involve us
In the difficulties and dangers by which
he is confronted and from which we are
free We need neither her advice nor
her assistance. The. praises which she
now bestows on us, were they sincere,
would be superfluous; but since they are
given with the design of drawing us Into
an imperialistic policy and troublesome
entanglements, they are insidious and In
sulting. Our wisest statesmen have al
ways been opposed to militarism as a
menace to our liberty. We want nothing
more than the nucleus of an army, noth
ing which shall serve as a means of con
quest at home or abroad; and, for my
own part, I think a powerful navy a dan
ger rather than a protection. We have
nothing to fear even from a coalition of
the powers of Europe, were such a thing
possible.
With ourselves, as in the rest of the
civilized world,- there is a drift toward
socialism. We must face the great prob
lems thus raised with faith In our political
principles and with confidence in the good
sense and honesty of the people. To seek
refuge in the intervention of a standing
army, under the command of a quasi-dlc-
itor. or emperor, is to enter the wav of
anarchy and ruin.
When the American people resolved not
to hold what they never intended to take
possession of, they will have little diffi
culty in finding a solution of this Philip
nine difficulty. Above all. let them not
be misled by vanity; let them not harken
to the siren voice of English flattery; let
them not stop to think what other na
tions shall say, but let them, as becomes
a great, a free and enlightened people.
be self-directed, holding in view only
such alms and ends as are wise, lust and
conducive to the permanent welfare and
highest interest of the republic.
ILL-LUCK PURSUES ONE,
FORTUNE THE OTHER
, Contrast in the Careers of Two Men
Operating in Shasta County
Mines.
REDDING, April 30.-The sale of the
Capital mine brings Into prominence the
striking contrast in the luck of two men
[ who have been operating in the mines of
, Shasta County. One has the record of
j selling two fortunes for a tithe of their
J value, and the other of buying two bank
accounts for a song.
Last night Sheriff Behrens accepted
â  first money on the Capital mine, the bal
ance to be paid on Saturday. To-day the
i Sheriff received news from his superin
l tendent that a rich two-foot ledge had
! been struck in the Capital, and his su
, perintendent advised him not to sell the
property short of $10,000. The sale had
: already been effected for $3000. The mine
i Is located a mile and a half from Kes
: Wick. *
Not long ago Sheriff Behrenrf 1 and a
partner Bold the Mount Shasta mine for
| $6000. It has since produced many times
that amount and to-day $100,000 could not
touch it. Ninety men are employed in the
mine and 'twelve six-horse teams are
; hauling high grade ore to the smelter. So
much for Sheriff Behrens' luck.
The buyer of the Capital mine, which
trebled in value in one night, is Colonel
J. H. Moore. A short time ago Moore
bought a third Interest in what was
afterward named the Daisy Belle mine,
located near Furnaceville. ' He paid $'">
for his interest and in a few days a strike
was made which netted the owners $4500
within a week.
Eleven hundred acres of auriferous
gravel lands along the Trinity River in
the neighborhood of Bragdon have just
been bonded to an Eastern company for
$23,000.
Federation in Australia.
LONDON, April 29.â At a meeting of the
Empire League held at Osborne to-day
the Agent General for Tasmania, after
predicting the speedy and unanimous
adoption by Parliament of the Australian
federation bill, added the important an
nouncement that recently he had been
able to inform the Secretary of State for
the Colonies, Joseph Chamberlain, that
three Australian colonies are framing
tariff bills differentiating in favor of Brit
ish manufactures and against imports
from other countries. The Agent General
also alluded to Australia's gratification
at the present cordiality of the relations
between Great Britain and the United
States.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, MAY 1, 1899.
WILSON CAUGHT
AT SUITS CRUZ
Mulatto Criminal
More in Jail. ..- ', h
SEEN RIDING ON A TRAIN;
WAS ATTEMPTING TO REACH
MEXICO.
The Suspected Strangler Admits His
Identity and Will Be Returned
to San Francisco for
Trial.
-
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA CRUZ, April 30.â Harry Wil
son, the mulatto criminal who is
wanted in San Francisco for burglary,
and who Is suspected of having
strangled May McDermott and Bertha
Paradis, was captured this noon by
Chief of Police Rawle, and is behind the
bars in the County Jail. Credit for
this capture is due to Lee Rawle, son of
the Chief of Police. Young Rawle is
employed as watchman at Capitola,
four miles from this city. Last even
ing he was at Capitola Station as the
train passed through for Santa Cruz.
Rawle happened to glance toward, a car
window and got a glimpse of the face
of a colored man he thought resembled
the description of Wilson. He tele
phoned his father, the Chief of Police,
who notified other officers, and a look
out was kept the entire. night.
This morning shortly before 6 o'clock
young Rawle made the trip from Capi
tola on his bicycle, and started in
search of the criminal. He found his
man in the "O. X.," a 15-cent restau
rant, and told Wilson he was an offi
cer. Wilson seemed to be much per
turbed. He told the Chief his name
was Harrison, and that he was from
Watsonvllle, where he had been at
work for the past two years. He
could not give the names of any per
sons from -Watsonvllle, and the Chief
accompanied him up the street, where
they met a Watsonville man who failed
to recognize the mulatto.
As the City Prison was not considered
safe, Sheriff Besse was sent for, and
Wilson was placed in- a cell in the
County. Jail. He again said his name
was Harrison.
Chief Lees of San Francisco was tele
phoned to, and Rawle, after getting a
complete description, was positive he
had the right man. He returned to the
Jail, got Wilson into a private room
and told the fellow he knew his name
was Wilson, and there was 'no use
denying it. Wilson then gave in, say
ing:
"My name is Wilson and I am the
man you want."
He said if he had not been caught he
would have left to-night on the steamer
for Mexico. A telegram was sent to
chief Lees, telling Of Wilson's con
fession, and the following response was
received by wire:
"Detective Gibson left 4 o'clock train
with warrant for Wilson."
Gibson arrived this evening on the 8
o'clock train, and will leave to-morrow
morning with his prisoner.
Chief Lees received a dispatch yester
day! morning from Chief of Police
Rawle' of Santa Cruz that a colored
man answering the description of Harry
"Wilson, the suspected strangler. who
was released on straw bonds Thursday
night by Clerk Kelly of Judge Gra
ham's court, had been seen in his baili
wick.
The Chief at once telephoned to
Rawle to take the suspect into custody
and endeavor to get him to confess that
he was the right man. Detective Ed
Gibson was instructed by the Chief to
take the 4 o'clock train for Santa Cruz,
in the hope that the man was Wilson.
After Gibson had left the Chief was
gratified to receive a dispatch from
Rawle that the man had been arrested
and admitted that he was Wilson. He
will be brought back by Gibson this
morning. _________________________________ ,

Bwlneford of Alaska left to-day for

thai c. .lies gold and copper in highly

properties in that district. Principal
among these is the Sea Level mine, which
has uncovered several ledges of free mill
ing, ore running from $18 up to thousands
of dollars per ton. The last quartz speci
mens received contain small pieces of al
most pure gold in such quantities as to
make a ton or such ore worth over $100,

OF SCURVY
AND FROST
! Sixty,. Physical Wrecks Re
turn From the Copper

DEAD LEFT BEHIND
s
Oakland Miner's Vain Attempt to
Transport His Siok Partner
to the Coast. Â«
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SEATTLE, Wash., April Sixty pros
pectors arrived here to-day from Copper
I River, Alaska, on the steamer Excelsior.
! They brought with them a repetition of
i the story of privation and death and
many bore evidence on their bodies of the
i ravages of scurvy and frost. A man
I named Young hobbled off the vessel on
! the stumps of two legs. Another, named
I Alex Powell, had but one foot.
F. C. Goodwin of Oakland, Cal., whose
legs are black with scurvy, and whose
knees are bruised by contact with the ice,
tells an interesting story of his experi
ences, which were similar to that of oth
ers. While camped in the interior his
partner, Wesley Scheidecker, of Grand
Ridge, 111., fell sick with the scurvy and
soon became unable to travel. Goodwin
strapped him on a sled and started to the
coast. After four days of almost her
culean labor he arrived at the mouth of
the Chitna River, but his partner was
- frozen stark and stiff to his sled. He
buried him in the snow.
Continuing his journey to the coast, he
came across a tent in which there were
two sick men with scurvy. They were
Dixon Hutton of Memphis and T. B, Raw
lins of Little Rock, Ark. Another man
happened along at the same time, and
he' and Goodwin put Rawlins and Hut
ton on a sled and dragged them to the
coast.
Just before the Excelsior left Valdes a
big slide was reported on the glacier,
but no particulars were learned. It was
rumored that a party of prospectors were
coming from the interior, and it was
feared they might have been caught in
the slide. H&Sil
The Government expedition under Cap
tain Abercromble was arranging to be
gin the construction of a road Into the
interior when the Excelsior left. Relief
parties with provisions and anti-scorbu
tics had been sent into the interior.

SLIGHT SHOCK
OF EARTHQUAKE
California Gets Mildly
Jarred.
â i
TEMBLOR DOES NO DAMAGE
IT OCCURS UNDER UNUSUAL
CONDITIONS. ,
Is Accompanied by a Heavy
. Downpour of Rain in Some

Special Dispatch to The Call.
SALINAS, April Salinas experienced
an earthquake shock this afternoon at
about 2:45 o'clock, under weather condi
tions 'wherein such disturbances are un
usual. Light rain had been falling for
some time and during the disturbance the
downpour was quite heavy, although no
damage was done to buildings. There
were two seperate and distinct shocks,
each of several seconds duration, the
vibrations being from southwest to north
east. The Salinas dummy line train,
which leaves this city at 2:45 o'clock for
Spreckelsville,' was rounding the curve at
Main and San Luis streets and the engine
jumped the track, but no particular dam
age was done, as the locomotive was run
ning, at low speed. Reports from the
Spreckels factory near this city are to the
effect that some of the machinery not yet
permanently placed into position was
jostled considerably.
â¢ MONTEREY, April 30.-A temblor that
shook this entire section of ' Monterey
County was felt at about 2:45 o'clock this
afternoon. The vibrations were appar
ently from west to east, although that is
not positively known, as no person here
abouts has instruments by which the
direction could haH; been ascertained.
The shock was the heaviest at Del Monte
and continued some seconds longer there
than elsewhere. No damage resulted. At
Pacific Grove two distinct shocks were
experienced, the second ; following the first
after an interval of two or three seconds
and being more severe. Rain has been
falling since noon to-day, a weather con
dition never before known to accompany
an earthquake. a

threatening.

JUST A SLIGHT SHOCK.
"Not Worth Recording," Says Pro
fessor Burckhalter.
San Francisco was shaken by an earth- {
quake yesterday afternoon, the seismic
disturbance lasting about ten seconds and j
consisting of two distinct shocks with
Intermediate vibrations of the earth. The i
first shock was felt at about 2:45 p. m.,
and while people were wondering whether
it was really an earthquake the vibrations
nearly ceased, and then the temblor
seemed to take a fresh grip and with a
final shake that rattled the windows of j
buildings all over town, subsided. It was ;
a very ordinary "quake," and although :
felt In all parts of the city caused not
the slightest damage so far as known. In
fact, the disturbance was so slight that
many were not aware of the presence of
the subterranean visitor until after the
shaking process had been concluded and
normal conditions again prevailed.
The earthquake was felt on the other
side of the bay, but the scientists in
Oakland and Berkeley who recorded the
seismic vibrations apeak of it in a tone of
disgust as an Insignificant affair and
hardly worthy of their attention. Profes
sor Charles Burckhalter, astronomer in
charge of the Chabot Observatory, in Oak
land, was in th* act of removing a smoked
glass from the seismograph which regis
ters the pranks of the temblors when a
Call reporter arrived there.
"Why, it really didn't amount to much,"
said he. "l won't waste a plate on it.
It was only one of those chatters, an.l
the earth really did not gyrate. No
doubt it came a long distance. 1 wouldn't '
say.it was slight, but lightâ that's bet- i
ter. The ", exact time was 2 hours, 41
minutes, 29 seconds.' its duration was ten
seconds, and the vibrations were from !
southeast to northwest." BaPtiMEfs
The clock of the seismograph at the
Students' Observatory in Berkeley
showed the earthquake to have passed
hy the university town at 2 hours, 41
minutes and 30 seconds past noon. All
the instruments gave excellent records,
but the shock was so slight that Dr.
â  Arhln O. Leuschner, the â¢" director-in
â¢ ' charge was inclined to rate it as of very
' little Importance unless reports should
show the disturbance to have been gen
eral The principal motion was toward
the west with a slight motion also to the
north By the Rossl-Forel scale its in
tensity would be measured by the Roman :
numeral it. The first shock lasted two
seconds, being succeeded by a number of
dvlng-out vibrations continuing for twelve
seconds. The latter were so slight that i
only the instruments could have detected
them. . __.___,__, .__
The principal shock consisted during the
first half second of three distinct vibra
tions causin" a total displacement of the
ground of one-twenty-fifth of an inch.
The corresponding northerly displace
ment amounted to but One-fiftieth of an
inch These figures were identical with
all the observatory Instruments.

WEDDED HAPPINESS
SAN JOSE, April 30.-On April 23, 1549, in Bucyrus, Oxford County, Ohio,
Wayne B. Rogers and Sarah Burgrove were married. Last Sunday, Apra
23, they celebrated the fiftieth anniversary of their marriage at their home,
seven miles south of San Jose.
Mr. and Mrs. Rogers are among the pioneers, having arrived in San Jose
on Christmas, 1852, For over forty-six years they have lived in the same house
on the Santa Teresa Rancho. __E_B
Both received their friends and callers in a manner that showed they still
have hopes of many years of life and the same degree of happiness with which
they have been blessed. All of their children, with their husbands and wives,
and" the grandchildren were present.
Eleven children were born to the couple. Five girls and one boy are living.
Two children died oefore reaching California and three since. Those living are:
Mrs. Emily J. Colombet, Mrs. Amanda C. Brandon, Mrs. Lily Singleton, Miss Ida
Rogers. Mrs. Delia Lester and George L. Rogers. The grandchildren are: Mrs.
Clementina Struven of San Francisco, Miss Charlotte Colombet, Master Charles
W. Colombet and F. C. Brandon of San Jose.

CHRISTIAN CONGREGATION
DEDICATES A CHURCH
Stockton House of Worship Erected
Through the Efforts of a
Clergyman.
STOCKTON. April 30.â The handsome
house of worship of .the First Christian
Church congregation was dedicated to
day, the occasion being marked by a
scries of services extending over the en
tire day. The edifice is a frame one, but
built on lines of substantiality and
beauty. It is a monument to the two
years' indefatigable effort^ of Rev.
Thomas A. Boyer, in building up the ma
terial as v w6ll as the spiritual Interests of
the congregation. The interior is bright
and cozy, with a seating capacity of 700.
The decorations were simple but pretty.
The pulpit is at the southwest cor
ner of the auditorium, and behind
it is a baptistery hidden by sliding
doors. There are many new accommoda
tions for candidates for baptism, such as
dressing rooms and screened exits. The
seating both on the ground floor and gal
lery is ranged in a circle facing the pul
pit. In addition to the regular audience
room there are Sunday-school and class
rooms.
Rev. Dr. Boyer was assisted at tiy
morning service by Rev. Mr. Martin of
Fresno and Rev. Mr. McHatton of Santa
Cruz, denominational associates. There
was a programme of unusual excellence,
several leading vocalists of the city par
ticipating. At the afternoon service all
of the evangelical church pastors partici
pated and felicitated the pastor and con
gregation. .â â¢Â»â¢
The church is free from debt and has
one of the finest locations in the city, at
the southwest corner of Lindsay and
California streets.

A NOTE FROM ANDREE
Reported Discovery Made on a Polar
Ice I toe Found Off Iceland's
Coast.
NEW YORK, April 30.â cable to the

land that in some polar ice in the neigh

a bottle has been found containing a note
from Professor Andree. The bottle, which
was frozen in the ice, was found by a

way. The text will not be known until
the next mall from Iceland and - . these
mails reach here at uncertain intervals
about six times a, year.

VETERAN OF THE SEA
CALLED BY DEATH
NEW YORK, April 30.â Haverhill
(Mass.) special to the Herald says: Cap
tain E. W. Reed, a retired sea captain,
died at his home in this city last evening.
Captain Reed was commander of the
ship T. F. Oakes which arrived in New
York two years ago, scurvy afflicted, after
a memorable voyage of 259 days from
Shanghail, during which the vessel en
countered two typhoons. Captain Reed
was stricken by an apoplectic stroke on
the voyage and his wife took command of
the vessel. Because of their heroism they
were presented with Lloyds rr - "als.
The captain was we., known in shipping
circles on both continents, having fol
lowed the sea for many years. He leaves
a widow and one son.

CAR JUMPSTHE TRACK
Running Down Grade at a Furious

Special Dispatch to The* Call.
HESTER, X. V., April 30.â Three
persons ere killed and more than a
dozen seriously Injured and fifty less
seriously hurt, as the result of a wreck
this afternoon on the Rochester and
Lake Ontario Railroad, better known
as the Bay Railroad at Rosehbauer's
Corners, about one and a half miles
north of the city line, when two cars
of an excursion train filled with pas
sengers left the track while rounding
a curve at full speed and were com
pletely wrecked. The dead:
JOHN HELBERG, aged 20, died at
hospital.
J. TIERNEY, aged 24, died at hos
pital.
UNKNOWN MAN. died after being
removed from he wreck.
The seriously injured: Julia Sullivan,
aged 18, fractured collar bone; George
Brass aged 36, compound fracture of
th*- forearm; Emil Steingraber. aged
22, back badly injured; Otto Hahnke.
aged 21, both legs badly bruised and
bones of right hand broken; William
Goodman, aged _. years, severe scalp
wound; Emil Schram, aged IS years,
small bones of foot broken; John Bier
shell, aged 42. compound fracture of
leg; Emma Tueselbe'of, aged IT. nose
broken, face terribly lacerated; James
Lombard, internal injury, may prove
fatal; J. F. Moore, H. 1.. Boric. Â»scar
Dorcshal, William Doerer, C. R. Rhine
hart, John Sullivan, Charles Warner,
Joseph Zimmer. The two latter may
die.
The slightly injured are: Charles
Darrow, A. F. Burnham, Henro Km-li
lin, Valentine Lochner, dm Russin
ger, Henry Bother, Frederick Gold
worth. Charles Nachaler, John 11.
Fredericks, John Fleming, Frederick
Schelberg, Albert Tippens; Frederick J.
Weihnert. W. S. Woodruff, John De
Roller. Joseph Hicks, .Mrs. John Hicks,
injun internally.
Besides these, at least twenty-five
are cut about the face and otherwise
injured. They went home without giv
ing their names.
The train, which was crowded with
excursionists bound for different points
along the shores of Lake Ontario in th-
Irondequoit Bay district, left tin- Bay
Station iii this- cit.v at 2:41 o'clock this
afternoon, about fifteen minutes late.
Every seat in the coaches was filled
and a large number of people * ere
islanding on the forms. The train
was made up of an engine, me closed
and two open coaches. There is a
grade about 100 yards from the corner
of North avenue and Ridge road, and
when this grade was reached the speed
of the I rain increased perceptibly.
Down the grade went th*- train, its
speed increasing constantly. When
the far curve was reached the engine
rounded it with startling rapidity.
Next to the engine was the closed car
riage, divided into two compartments, j
and when it ached the curve it veered j
over to the north side of the road
around the curve on the left wheels.
It had gone but a part of the bend in
the track when it left the rails and
shot straight ahead. There was a sud
den crash and the couplings between
the engine and coach *vere severed and
the coach again veered over and ;
plowed through the earth for several
feet before dropping on its side in the ;
front of th*- Ridge Road Hotel.
The closed coach which was over- i
turned had a partition that divided :
the front part from the rear. Men and
women were thrown into a jumbled
mass over against the roof of the car i
nnd were pinioned under the seats and
the partitions in th- car, which was
torn to splinters.
John Hilborg was standing on the
platform and was caught between the
two cars and crushed to death.
The work of rescue was energetically I
carried on, and as sunn as each sufferer
was taken out he was sent to the hos
pital.

SLAIN AS
HE CHASES
A BANDIT
Ogden's Captain of Police
Recklessly Courts
Death.
ONE OUTLAW KILLED
While Attempting to Capture His
Companion Alive the Officer
Is Shot.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
OGDEN, Utah, April Captain of
| Po.ice William A. Brown was killed
I this afternoon by a crook whom
ihe sought to arrest. Last night citi-
I zens of Brigham City were held up
|by two men, and at about 10 o'clock
the cutthroats were found near the Hot
Springs, ten miles north of Ogden. The
Sheriff of Box Elder County and his
| deputies had a running fight with them,
! during which time about forty shots were
! fired without a single one taking effect.
[ During the light the robbers threw away
their plunder. They escaped to the moun
tains.
The Sheriff telephoned to ngden for
help. Sheriff Layne, Deputies Belknap
and Bailey and Captain Brown of the po
lice force went out at 4 o'clock this morn
ing, and at 3 o'clock this afternoon they
had the two robbers entrapped in the
mountains. At the time the Sheiff's posse
was about l"" feel above the men and
looking clown on their hiding place. The
officers -all. d upon them to surrender and
the answer was a half-dozen bullets.
Three members of the posseâ Sheriff Cor
don of Box Elder, Deputy Belknap of
Ogden and Captain Brownâ then secured
a position behind some rocks and called
again for the outaws to surrender. The
elder of the two cried out, "We'll kill you
first." md fired again.
The three officers then took deliberate
aim and killed one of the outlaws. The
remaining outlaw started to run. Brown
cried out that he would capture him alive
and started toward him. Both were run
ning and Brown was gaining on the rob
ber, when he turned and fired, the bullet
passing through Brown's body from right
to left and penetrating the heart.
As soon as Brown fell the robber threw
up both hands and surrendered.
Captain Brown was a member of the
police force for ten years, and has been
a captain but three weeks. He had a
record for a larger number of arrests
than any other member of the force. For
years li,- was on the Union Depot heat.
and he was known to almost every
traveler who ever came through the city.
He was a brother-in-law of ex-Senator
Frank J. Cannon.
The dead robber was named 'William
Morgan and hailed from Chicago. The
younger of the two Is but IS years old.
His story is that the two were brothers.
The dead" robber was about 35 years old.
There is a rumor here to the effect that
a large party is en route to Brigham City

FORESTS MENACED I
BY AN INSECT PEST
Government Agent Investigating in
Northern California and South
ern Oregon. |
SISSON. April 30.â A. D. Hopkins, pro- :
fessor of entomology, of Washington. D.
C, has been detailed by the Government
to investigate the destruction of timber
at Grants Pass. Or., by the insect or
borer that is becoming very destructive
in that locality. In speaking of his mis
sion Professor Hopkins said that through I
West Virginia and other Middle and j
Southern States over an area of 75.000 i
square miles the forests have been wholly j
destroyed by a borer or pest which he
thinks is the same as is reported from
this locality, and the Government is tak
ing active measures to ascertain the hab
its, character and extent of propagation
of the insect and to endeavor to destroy
It. In Squaw Valley he procured some
very fine specimens of a grub or borer |
and its workings. He expressed the belief i
that the healthy timber in this locality
was still free from the pest, owing to;
there being so much timber felled and
manufactured into wood and lumber in
this section that the 'nsect has other ma
terial in the way of stumps and old logs,
which it prefers to work on. There is
no immediate danger of the insect attack
ing the virgin forests and healthy tim
ber.

To Enlist More Naval Apprentices.
NEW YORK, April 30.â A Washington
speed to the Herald says: Rear Admiral
Crownlnshield, Chief of the Bureau of
Navigation, has given instructions to the
commanding officer of the Pensacola to
enlist boys as naval apprentices. The
Pensacola will accommodate 200 appren
tices, and the Adams is now on a cruise
with about the same number on board.
Barracks are being erected on Yerba
Buena Island for 400 apprentices. It is
the intention to keep half of the 800 boys
enlisted on the Pacific Coast afloat and
the remainder ashore.

In California the
clay will be one of
special interest. It
was born here and
only here Is It recog
nized as a true na
tional holiday, for
only in this State
has the Government
sanctioned the firing
of the admiral's sa
lute of seventeen
guns, which will
form the warlike
element of the cele
bration. The re
mainder will be left
to the orator, the
singer, the band and
the crowds, and the
countless flags which
will wave the freer
from the victory won
for them in the
southern seas. The
day was the sugges
tion of the Sons of
Vermont. There was
a meeting of the or
der in this city last
January and the
proposition of as
sisting the Sons of
Veterans in celebrat
ing Bunker Hill day
In June was dis
cussed. It had al
most been decided to
join in that event
when It was sug
gested that the first
of May be made a
day of their own,
just such a day as
they had been look
ing for to give an
excuse for a celebra
tionâand what more
glorious cause than
the brilliant achieve
ment of Admiral
Dewey. The Idea
took at once and be
fore a week had
passed the president
of the order, Judge
E. A. Belcher, had
communicated with
all the branches in
the State and had
written to most of
those throughout the
country, and from
every side came the most favorable in
dorsements of the plan. And In recogni
tion of the origin of the idea of Dewey
day, when the Government was appealed
to to give military sanction to the cele
bration, a salute was ordered fired in this
Stateâ a favor granted to no other appli
cant.
The principal celebration will be- at
Sunset Park, in Santa Cruz County. The
Sons of Vermont will take charge of the
day there, and there will be speeches and
music and flags and enthusiasm to last
the day and perhaps a good part of the
night. * Judge Belcher will open proceed
ings with a few words of welcome, and
then will follow a programme of patriot
ism appropriate and inspiring.
It is announced that the celebration in
the Santa Cruz Mountains will take place
rain or shine. There Is a big pavilion at
the park, anyway, and there is no need
of any one getting wet, even should it
rain Its hardest.
The start will be made from the foot of
Market street. Those who go will have
to take the 8:45 narrow-gauge boat.
Round-trip tickets will be $1 and half that
for children under 12 years of age. They
can be purchased at tne ferry after half
past 7 this morning. The return will be
at such time as to land the people in the
city early in the evening.
Around the city the day will be ob
served in a variety of ways, but mostly in
the increase in the number of attractions
which invite people to stay out in the
air. The park will be the scene of the
prettiest ceremony of the day. With the
band playing martial airs the silken flag
made by the sailors of the McCul
loch and presented to the city when that
vessel arrived here after the battle of
Manila will be raised to its place of su
premacy over all.
There will be picnics without limit, for
this is the picnic season, and one of the
fruits of Dewey's victory was to insure
freedom to the people. There is a good
part of the population of this city to
whom freedom means a picnic.
ARGONAUTS CELEBRATE
AT SCHUETZEN PARK
FIFTH ANNUAL PICNIC OF THE
FORTY-NINERS.
Jupiter Pluvius Regulates the Ele
ments and an Enjoyable Day Is
Spent in Games and
Dancing.
When the *49ers started for Schuetzen
Park, near San Rafael, on their fifth an
nual excursion and picnic yesterday it
was raining. But Jupiter Pluvius evi
dently holds the worshipers at the shrine
of the Argonauts in nigh esteem, for the
pilgrims received one of old Sol's genial
smiles as they disembarked on the Marin
County shore and the rest of the day was
theirs to enjoy.
The excursionists crowded all the boats
plying between this city and Tiburon dur
ing the morning and a few delayed their
departure until the afternoon. In all there
were fully two thousand people at the
park, where Fred Schumann had made
all necessary arrangements for their re
ception. The day was pleasantly whiled
been invited to be present and to take
part.
Children's celebration at Glen
Park. Parade and drill of school chil
dren at 10 o'clock and games in the
afternoon.
Baseball at Recreation Park at
2:30 p. m. between the Oakland and
San Francisco teams.
Grand reunion of the parishioners
of St. Francis' parish at Shell Mound
Park. Band will leave on the 9
o'clock boat.
Picnic of employes of the Califor
nia Northwestern Railway Company
at Mirabel Park.
By TOM GREGORY.
Glen Park will be another center of en
thusiasm, for there the school children
will hold sway. There will be a parade of
scholars and if expectations are realized
there will be fully three thousand chil
dren in line. There will also be games
and music. The entertainment will com
mence at 10 o'clock.
The salute of seventeen guns will be
fired from the training ship Pensacola,
from Aleatraz and from the Presidio.
That from the Pensacola will be at 8
o'clock and from the land batteries at 12.
The celebration commenced as early as
last evening, when a number of the local
ministers took as their, texts for the Sun
day evening sermons something pertain
ing to the great victory and the man who
won it.
Rev. F. C. Lee of the California Meth
odist Episcopal Church spoke on "Admir
able Admiral Dewey. ' In the course of
his remarks he said:
All hail Dewey! All hail Dewey Day! An
other star ls added to our galaxy of heroes.
Among the Sabbaths of our national life a
new day has dawned. I believe there are
some things worth fighting for, and liberty â
civil and religiousâ ls one of them. There Is
such a thing as righteous warfare, and for
the righteousness of our wars as a nation we
challenge the world for as clean a record.
Think as you may, I for my part do not
believe that all the good and great men are
dead and buried. I believe there is just as
good patriotism and just as much of lt ln
the world to-day as ln any previous day. I
believe there is Just as much honor. Just as
much courage. Just as much purity and vir
tue. Just as much brain and brawn In our
land and nation this hour as there was in
'76 or ln '63. Heroism and patriotism are not
extinct virtues, and let no man, with the
record of Santiago and Manila Bay before him.
dare to write opposite these obsolete!
With our pools and combines, trusts and cor
ners ln wheat, sugar, oil, coal, our feverish
anxiety for the latest mining and stock quo
tations, it may look to a Spaniard or a Fili
pino that we worship the "Golden Calf," and
while down on our knees in worship scram
bling for the mighty dollar. But when the
trumpet of war sounds the call to arms-
I away in dancing in the large pavilion,
! games of various kinds and feasting,
most of the picnickers carrying along well
I filled baskets, which were very light bur
dens on the homeward trip.

a series of races in which young and old
alike took part and furnished the specta
tors with a deal of amusement. The re
sults were as follows:

when a Secretaiy of
the Navy like. Roose
velt, millionaires and

aires, like Astor and
Fish, enlist and go to

to our very finder tips,
we rally round the

And In the hour of

beats true, while thrk-e
10,000 lives are loyal
even unto death. If
history has any lesson
for us it is that un
usual conditions pro
duce great characters,
and that God always
has the man for tic
hour. So while other
â  generations have had
their Moses, their
Charlemagne, Crom
well, Washington,
Wellington, we have
our Shatter, our Schley
and our Dewey! Every
epoch and evsry era
brings its own inspira
tionâthe man, or men,
fitted for the times.
Let us then to-mor
row reverse the old
senseless and heartless
custom of waiting un
til our heroes are six
feet under ground be
fore we sing their
praise or wreath the
laurel. A kind word
while living is worth a
ton of granite when
dead.
Let Dewey day be
celebrated with burst
if music and roar of
gun. Let a . grateful
nation shout out its
praises so long and
loud that our words of
gratitude and good
cheer will go echoing
across the Pacific from
the Golden Gate to
Hawaii and frcm Ha
waii to Manila, reach
ing the ears and glad
dening the heart of
our Admiral â whose

Admiral Dewey, as
an American citizen I
salute you. May you
"never give up the
ship" for the White
House; the bridge for
th*' Presidential chair!
Dr. Charles Ed
ward Locke's sub
ject at the Central
Episcopal Church
last evening was
"D ewe y's Victory
and the New Prob-
lems and Opportuni
ties It Brings to
Church and State."
His text was "God
shall Mess us; and
all the ends of the
earth shall fear
h i m." Psalms
Lxvii:7.
__. Dr. Locke said:
This evening closes the most memorable year
of this generation. Between May 1. 1898, "and
to-night events have occurred which are chang
ing the policies of the nations and swinging
portals wide open to the progress of truth.
National expansion is Inevitable; It is thrust
upon us. The Philippines are ours as part of
the victory of war. The hands do not move
backward upon the dial. It would have been
cowardly to leave the Islands in the palsied grip
of Spain, and It would be cruel to leave them
to semi-savage self-government: and it would
be puerile to refuse to bear the obligations
which their retention will Impose upon us as
a people.
To take these islands and Christianize and
civilize them Is our response to to refrain of
the ages, "Westward, ho! the star of empire
takes Its way!"
Dewey's victory, which Is to be celebrated to
morrow, has also brought new and Imposing
opportunities to the church. The church la to
carry the pure gospel and teachings of Christ
to multitudes of people to whom the Son of
God has been misrepresented. The corruption
of the Philippine Islands Is Indescribable.
Loathsome orgies of Impurity have cursed the
people. Official rapacity and priestly despotism
have enslaved the inhabitants. I have details
of the depravity of the teachers and leaders
of the people that would be Indelicate to men
tion before this audience.
It ls the church's duty to go promptly into
these our new possessions, because here for
several hundred years has been exemplified the
fact that a Christian civilization without our
Christ does not civilize. And If avaricious and
greedy men are not preceded and followed by
Christian workers lt would be better for the
Filipinos to relapse Into paganism.
Abject heathenism ls better than modern
civilization without Christ. A Market-street
firm In this city makes a boast that the largest
consignment of beer ever made has just gone
to the Philippines. The excrescences of our
civilization are a curse to any people.
The cross must go with commerce. The ma
chinery of our methods of living and doing
destroys life unless there ls the great balance
-wheel of faith In ami love for Christ.
The last of the celebration will be
flashed from the slopes of Twin Peaks,
where the improvement clubs of the mis
sion will keep bonfires blazing until after
midnight.

Preached at St. John's Church.
Rev. Dr. Brant of Boston preached an
inspiring sermon yesterday morning be
fore the congregation of St. John's Epis
copal Church. His text was: "Howbeit
thai was not tirst which is spiritual, but
that which is natural, and afterward,
that which is spiritual." He spoke
of spiritual proceeding from the natural
as exemplified in the various affairs of
life, and the necessity of wrestling with
our difficulties for the glory of that which
shall come after.
In the evening the rector. Dr. E. P.
Spalding, was the preacher, the subject
being, "The Unchangeable God."

ips, one of the best known vineyardists

A CLEAR COMMERCIAL SKY.
THE domestic and foreign trade of the United
States, as shown by the weekly exports, is ex
ceptionally prosperous. This is the season
when the export trade generally falls to its minimum
for the year, and in spite of this the exports of manu
factured products for the month of March were $36,
--000,000, or 35 per cent of all the exports, this fine
showing largely compensating for the falling off in
the shipments of cereals and other natural products.
This proportion has seldom if ever been touched be
fore, and it is a striking illustration of the great ex
pansion of our foreign trade. The exports of wheat
this crop year have been considerably less than during
the same period in 1898, the figures being 96.290,000
bushels, against 195.592,000 bushels. The bank clear
ings show no falling off in general business, those for
last week being 65.7 per cent larger than for the same
week in 1898, and New Orleans nd Minneapolis
were the only cities of any size to show a decrease.
The gain in New York was 91.7 per cent, in Boston
69.3 per cent, in Pittsburg 76 per cent, and 51 * on.
The commercial failures were 184. against 245 for the
corresponding week last year. There arc no signs
of a falling off in domestic trade anywhere, though the
demand for general merchandise is lighter, as is usual
at this season of the year, when the spring trade is
over and the summer trade has hardly begun. The
retail business of the country, however, is reported
good, being assisted by favorable weather.
The staples remain about as previously reported.
Wool is quieter but steady, and sales of foreign de
scriptions for export are still being made. Cop
per has ruled very firm at an advance. Leather is
also strong, largely owing to the small stocks of hides
available. Wheat is unsettled, but prices stand about
where they have been for some little time. Lumber
continues firm. Iron and steel are quieter as far as
new business is concerned, but prices continue strong
and the margin of profit is still good.
hi? remarkable hew well business stands the strain
of foreign complications. It was feared a week or
two ago that the strained relations in Samoa and the
Philippines would have an adverse effect, but thus far
none has been apparent. It was the same through
out the Spanish war. No matter what happened
abroad the business of the country went on expand
ing, with hardly a ripple on the commercial stream.
It is an object lesson to other countries, as showing
the stability of American institutions and the perfect
confidence of the American people in their Govern
ment. We know that we are able to take care of
ourselves on land or sea, and in the midst of war go
on merchandising as if war were a mere side issue â
something to amuse the children, as it were.
There was not much excitement in Wall street dur
ing the week. The continued easing of the money
market, larger supplies if funds and the disposition
on the part of the banks and money lenders to look
more favorably upon industrials as collateral had a
pood effect and the feeling was decidedly better. The
only drawbacks were the uncertainty regarding the
crops and a slackening of public interest in the
market, which made itself apparent in a falling off in
the volume of transactions. Bonds again came to the
front in preference to stock?, and were strong and in
demand.
Conditions in California continue brilliant. It is
resting to observe that in official reports of the
condition of the wheat crop the six principal winter
rv heat States make an average of 60 per cent, while
California is classed as perfect, or 100 per cent. This
means millions to us. and its importance cannot be
c\ stimated. If wheat is perfect the minor cereals
are likely to be equally promising, and they are. Fruit
conditions remain about the' same. With the single
exception of peaches the crop will be light, but there
will be no famine in any description, and the light
res': of the yield will result in higher prices and better
markets, besides leaving the field clear for the next
year. This season promises brilliantly for the fruit
grower and general farmer, and as a natural conse
quence for the merchant; and it is reasonably safe to
lay that 1899 will be one of the best all-round years
)he State has ever seen.
Massachusetts has* come to the conclusion that the
telephone business is a public service that ought to
be regulated by the public and not by a monopoly,
and a bill has been introduced into the Legislature
giving the State Gas and Electric Commission the
right to supervise it and fix prices. The conse
quence is the telephone company in Boston is crying
out that the good old State is getting to be as woolly
as Kansas.
.
The announcement is made that among the sup
plies the Government is to purchase for the Indians
this year re to be a number of lawn mowers, so the
next outbreak of the brave . will probably be one of
profanity because the Government did not provide
loldicrs to run the machines.
THE BATTLE OF MANILA BAY.
j r\ YEAR ago to-day, at a quarter to 8 o'clock in
i j\ the morning, while Admiral (then Commodore)
â  * Dewey and his forces were fortifying themselves
by a hearty breakfast for the second part of the battle
of Manila Bay, Colonel George A. Loud on the
revenue cutter Hugh McCulloch wrote in his diary
j the following words:
"This is Sunday and May day, and it will be an
j American date in history."
This prediction, made when the echoes of the ter
rible cannonade that had destroyed the flagship of the
Spanish admiral and wrought havoc on the Spanish
fleet and the Cavite battery had scarcely died away,
has been literally fulfilled. In the dead of the night
the American squadron had passed Corregidor
Island, and in the gray dawn it -had approached
within four miles of Manila. Five times it had
sailed in grand procession, describing a semi-circle,
and had poured jits terrific broadsides upon the
enemy, both in the water and upon the land. The
I. la de Cuba, to which the Spanish admiral had trans
ferred his flag, had been sunk. One torpedo-boat had
disappeared and another had been riddled under the
fire of the Olympia. When Colonel Loud wrote the
battle had been practically won, but at 20 minutes to
11 it was renewed, and by half-past 12 the Spanish
vessels were sunk or destroyed, Cavite had sur
rendered and Manila was at our mercy. Not an
American life had been lost and only five or six of a
gun crew on the Baltimore had been slightly
wounded by splinters.
I This is a brief record of a naval engagement that j
. humbled the pride of Spain, emblazoned our Occi- j
' dental flag with the splendors of the Orient, estab-
l ished a new epoch in naval warfare and enrolled Ad- i
: miral Dewey in the fraternity of the immortals. The j
sacrifice of life among the defeated Spaniards was
: comparatively small. But seven of their ships were
: swept from their chosen refuge, and their shore de
fenses, armed with powerful guns, reduced with a
precision and with a celerity that had never previously
been equaled. In every sense the victory was com
-1 plete. In the first actual test upon a large scale of
: modern vessels and modern armaments, in which me
. chanical adjustments play such a conspicuous part, the
United States led the powers of the world. Admiral
Dewey, with his broad intellect, with his scientific
education and trained experience, with his perfect
nerve and with his calm and prompt judgment, was
the very type of a great naval commander of the nine
teenth century. He made no mistakes, then or since,
either of omission or commission. The deep enthu- I
siasm of his countrymen, the plaudits of the nations;
have not shaken his self-poise. He remains where |
his honors were won, the appreciative recipient of !
Congressional ami popular distinction, avoiding the 'â 
rock of politics, upon which so many high reputa
tions have been wrecked, and illustrating his patriot
ism by the uncomplaining endurance of privations
and by the resolute fulfillment of duty. And no one
of the intelligent and brave officers and men who dis
played their superiority of race and of discipline has
been overlooked by their commander or will be for
gotten by their rejoicing fellow-citizens. All are in
cluded in the glory of this auspicious anniversary.
"Tho dominant blood that daunts the foe.
That laughs at odds and leaps to the blow-
It is Dewey's glory to-day, as Nelson's
A hundred years ago."
THE HUNTINGTON DICTATORSHIP-
T T has been the custom of Mr. C. P. Huntington
IT has been annual visit to Mr. State to deliver one
during his annual visit to this State to deliver one
speech, in which his imperial decrees were suc
cinctly registered. This year the latest phase of rail
road policy has been developed in a succession of ad
dresses by Mr. Huntington, in which his autocratic
character is boldly and incisively revealed.
He appears to entertain a sublime confidence in
himself and an unflinching determination to govern
both State and national policy. His blunt expres
sions, however, in the first place, conflict with estab
lished facts, and, in the second place, excite the
deepest hostility. If. in a few sentences on his own
behalf and representing his railroad corporations, he
would announce his withdrawal from all pretense of
supremacy over the law and over social, industrial and
political action, and then live, up to the declaration,
harmony would be restored and the railroad and the
public could labor together for that kind of prosperity
that would lift the State out of the slough of corrup
tion to a level corresponding with its geographical po
sition and its natural advantages.
But Mr. Huntington seems to be obstinately bent
upon rigid adherence to despotic conservatism, and
when, with the stern paternalism of William of Ger
many, he occasionally expresses his personal wish to
advance the public interests, prolonged and bitter ex
perience contradicts his statements.
In his address of Thursday night at the Palace
Hotel banquet he formulated his belief, in which the
intelligence of the world shares, "in the future of San
Francisco," and declared that the true interests of
the State and of the railroad were identical. This
proposition has never been seriously disputed except
by Mr. Huntington himself, whose definition of
mutuality of interest, like Lord Glosmore's concep
tion of liberty, means unqualified submission to rail
road demands and to railroad aggressions. With
marked consistency with his past conduct, but with
equally conspicuous inconsistency of sentiment, he
advocated Asiatic expansion, which would insure an
endless supply of cheap and degraded . labor, but
recognized the great necessity of California to be the
introduction of thrifty colonists. He even addressed
honeyed words to the unfortunate Finns, whose ex
cellent capacity for colonization will be generally con
ceded. But what immigrating spider would be de
sirous of entering the railroad web? Does Mr. Hun
tington remember the Dutch colony that was induced
to settle a few miles from Merced, which comprised
an unusual combination of intelligent, educated and
honest families, possessed of means, who transferred
themselves and their possessions with their Lares and
Penates to California? Those people were drawn
from their native land by glowing representations and
by flattering proposals. They brought with them the
industry and the integrity of their race and expended
large sums of money, established substantial homes,
erected ornamental and attractive structures for so-
cial purposes and commenced horticultural operations
upon an extensive and diversified scale. The result
was that, within a few years, their capital was lost and
their enterprise abandoned. Most of them were re
duced to poverty and the survivors returned to Hol
land. To-day their settlement is an uninhabited ruin.
The soil, thinly spread over the bedrock, would bring
no fruit to perfection, and the dead orchards and the
crumbling buildings are a ghastly memorial to fraud
and to greed.
White colonization is a prime necessity to this
State, but it must be promoted under public auspices.
It will not flourish through railroad methods.
There are many other striking points in the last
manifesto of Mr. Huntington. He indorsed in words,
as an excellent rule of mercantile life, supplementing
the payment of "legal taxes" by allowing a reason
able margin of profit to purchasers of goods, but he
omitted a reference, which the community will sup
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, MAY 1, 1899.
ply, to the fact that the railroad has always been the
principal tax-shirker on this coast and has rarely con
tributed to the liquidation of public indebtedness ex
cept under compulsion or through compromises that
produced the most unjust unequality.
California does need "more people" and our mer
chants "more customers," but our "logical develop
ment" can only be secured through efforts apart from
the railroad, unless Mr. Huntington reverses his
policy and renounces a dictatorship, to which Ameri
can communities will not submit.
THE DREYFUS CASE.
IT has been frequently announced in the press that
the decision of the Court of Cassation in the
Dreyfus case will be promulgated to-day. Wheth
er this statement is authoritative or not remains to
be seen. All civilized nations await the final decision
of the tribunal that represents the ultimate judg
ment of France with the deepest interest.- The real
question seems to be whether, under a government
nominally republican, the law can be gagged by
military influence.
An enormous mass of evidence has been taken and
most of it has been published. That the conviction
of Dreyfus by a court-martial was improper is the
very least that can be claimed under the additional
proof. The bordereau, containing treasonable in
formation for the benefit of Germany upon which
the prosecution was based, must certainly now be
discarded. Three out of five experts at the trial ex
pressed the opinion that it was in the handwriting
of Dreyfus, but one of these three, M. Charavay,
upon further consideration, has retracted his original
views, and the majority, therefore, now favor the ac
cused. If in other respects the trial had not been
grossly unfair, this fact alone ought to insure either
the release of Dreyfus or a new hearing.
Under the French method of reinvestigation it has
been insisted that there were other evidences of guilt,
not introduced before the court-martial or the in
troduction of which was studiously concealed at the
time, by which' the alleged crime was or. might have
been sustained. The widest latitude for the pre
sentation of this testimony has been allowed by the
Court of Cassation, which has considered an unlim
ited number of documents and many oral statements,
chiefly hearsay, matter of opinion and vague con
jecture.
The result is that the case against Dreyfus has
been materially weakened, and, in the opinion of
sound lawyers in France and elsewhere, his innocence
has been at least presumptively shown. His alleged
confession has not been proved. The secret dossier
upon which so much reliance was placed was almost
certainly an ingenious afterthought. The trial and
acquittal of Esterhazy, upon a charge of having
written the bordereau, was transparently a concocted
scheme to check the development of public opinion
in favor of Dreyfus. The confinement of Colonel
Picquart, who chivalrously assailed the proceedings
that had brought reproach upon France, is an obvi
ous persecution. The arbitrary punishment of Zola
was an attack upon free speech and upon the right
to express honest opinions, for which the only re
cent parallel to be found is the legislative onslaught
upon the California press at the instigation of the
railroad.
The law governing the Supreme Tribunal of
France lias been amended during the present year.
As it now stands the revision proceedings must re
sult in one of three judgments. It may be held that
the conviction was legal and just, but this can hardly
be anticipated. It is more likely that one of the re
maining alternatives will be adopted. The Court
may annul the sentence, declare Dreyfus innocent
and award him an indemnity, or it may determine that
a crime was perpetrated, with which he was not
legally connected, and direct a retrial before another
tribunal. The last form of decision would simply
recognize the evident fact that some one having ac
cess to the records of the Staff Department of the
French Army had committed a heinous offense, but
would not imply a judicial belief in the guilt of
Dreyfus.
It is earnestly to be hoped that the outcome of
the investigation may relieve France from the im
putations of injustice and cruelty that have impaired
her national standing, and measurably remedy an
apparent atrocity, against which more than three mil
lion Frenchmen have at last protested.
THE SIBERIAN RAILWAY.
RECENT statistics relating to the great Siberian
Railway show that the line will be something of
much more value than a mere military road.
As the line open for traffic is extended the amount
of its business increases so rapidly there is abundant
evidence to justify the conclusion that the railway is
needed to meet the demands of commerce and in
dustry and will repay the Russian Government even
if it were of no strategic value whatever.
Upon the western and central divisions of the great
road now in operation there were carried in 1896 less
than 175,000 passengers. In 1897 there were 413,000
and in 1898 fully 650,000. The present year, it is pre
dicted, will see the number increased to 1,000,000.
Equally marked has been the increase in freight. In
1896 it was scarcely 405,000,000 pounds; in 1898 it was
more than 1,475,000,000 pounds. These figures show
to what an extent Siberia is being colonized and its
resources developed.
This rapid increase of traffic is said to be surprising
to the Russians themselves. It is much greater than
the road was prepared for, and as a consequence
while the line is being pushed forward at the rate of
about two miles a day a sum equal to $9,000,000 has
been appropriated to lay heavier and better rails along
certain portions of the old track to fit it for the traffic
carried on upon it; and at the same time something
like $20,000,000 has been set apart for the construc
tion of branch lines as feeders.
It has been long foreseen that the opening of the
Siberian road will work a mighty change in the course
of the commerce between Europe and the Orient, but
it now appears it will work in Russia itself a much
greater change than was expected. It has broken up
the stagnation of ages and set large numbers of Rus
sian people to traveling in search of new homes. It
has proven that Siberia is really a rich country^ fitted
to become the home of a prosperous people. It will
bring the wheat lands of Siberia into close communi
cation with the parts of Russia where droughts are
common and will thus aid in diminishing the suffer
ing which follows the frequent famines. The rapidity
with which the traffic is increasing also has had the
effect of encouraging the Government in the work,
and the enterprise is now being prosecuted with more
vigor than ever.
Miss Viola Jeffres of Telegraph Hill, the author
of the Tom Bozzle letter, is wasting her time in a
cracker factory. Her gift of metaphor should have
no difficulty in finding a market. "My heart beats
for you," she wrote, "like a nanny-goat on a tin
roof." Match that if you can for a figure of speech.
Let every shop be closed to-day anu every starry
banner fly. There is but one Admiral Dewey in the
world â he is ours.

STRONG'S MASTERPIECE
GRACES BOHEMIA'S WALLS
Portrait of dames Â«M. .Hamilton as a .Highlander.
JOSEPH D. STRONG'S masterpiece, the picture which added the capstone
to his fame as a portrait painter, is to be shown among the art treasures
of the Bohemian Club. It was upon this picture Mr. Strong placed the last
touches of his brush, completing the work just before he was stricken
down. James M. Hamilton, a lifelong friend of the painter, posed for the
picture. The face, slightly idealized, bears all the characteristics of the gallant
Highlander.
There is an atmosphere of the braes about it. The figure shows all the poetry
and strong character of the Scotch race, while there is a glint in eye which
speaks of the subtle Scotch humor so ably exploited here recently by the Rev.
Dr. Watson. Mr. Hamilton was a favorite model for Mr. Strong. He often
said he could, when blindfolded, reproduce the kindly and familiar features of
his friend.
The picture is said by critics to be a companion in portraiture to Charles
Rollo Peters' wonderful landscape, "The Camp by the Cross." Both bear the
stamp of genius and both are the product of the great West, now commanding
such widespread attention in art, literature ana music.
Rain or shine the game will be played, be
ginning at 2:30 o'clock.

NEWS OF FOREIGN NAVIES.
France is expending $5,000,000 reboilerlng
and rearming the older Ironclads and
coast defense vessels.
The Russian cruiser building at Elbing,
Germany, has been named Novlk and is
to be completed by September, 1900. The
vessel is only 3000 tons displacement, but
is to steam twenty-five knots, and will
cost $2,152,500, exclusive of armaments.
Three of this type are contemplated to be
used as scouts.
Two cruisers about to be built for the
French navy, intended for courier and
outlook duty, are to be very fast. They |
are 394 feet in length. 44.6 feet beam and
4000 tons displacement The engines, of j
15,000 horsepower, are to give a speed of,
twenty-three knots, and the armament
will consist of eight 3 1-3-inch quick
firers, twelve 6-pounders and four 3
pounders. Their average cost is estimated
at $1,719,000.
There is great complaint at the Keyham
Naval Engineering School over the in
sufficiency of food given to the students.
After the noonday meal they only receive
weak tea. bread and butter, and at 9
o'clock in the evening each student gets
one bun. The engineer officers at the col
lege lay the blame upon the sailor officers,
who have charge of the Institution, and
are in favor of having the school taken
away from the control of the line and
turned over to the engineers and civilians.
â  It has been suggested by the London
Daily Graphic that the ships of the Brit
ish navy should have battle honor flags,
in the same way as the regiments have.
At the break of the poop and on the bar-
bettes or on the ring wheel appear
in large letters the names of the victories
in which ships of the same name have
taken part, but no ship has a flag bearing
such a record. Such a plan would no
doubt meet with approval if adopted in
the United States navy, and it would not
be records of victories gained by name
sakes.
The. London Times expresses some un
easiness regarding the use of water tube
boilers on naval vessels. When in 1895
the Belleville boiler was first introduced
in the Terrible and Powerful the Times
was not enthusiastic in favor of this new
departure, and it now expresses the opin
ion that the boiler accident on the Ter
rible is a blow to the advocates of this
type of boiler and that an Inquiry by the
Admiralty is needed to settle the question
of preference for the old style shell boiler
and the water tube type.
The several Russian battle-ships in
j course of construction all differ somewhat
jin size and general design. The Tsare
i witch, building under contract in France,
i is the largest, being 12,900 tons; Retrisam,
: building at ''ramus', is 12,700 tons, and
Pobieda (Victory), on the stocks at St.
j Petersburg, is 11,362 tons, and is 1300 tons
smaller but of the same general design as
the islabya and Peresviet. The Tsare
! witch will cost over $6,000,000, including
armament. One of the terms of the con
tract with the Cramps firm is that the
draught of the ship, ready for sea and with
1016 tons of coal aboard, is not to exceed
twenty-six feet. The consumption of coal
is also guarded against being too exces
sive and is limited to 1.7 p Hinds per horse
â  power per hour at a speed of ten knots.

over.

I'nited States Quarantine Officer Brooks

When he boarded the Sherman in the ;

Captain Lewis Baker and Steward Her
bert Edgar Passingham of the British
chip Mary A. TroojÂ» are on trial in Kng- I
land for maltreating and driving to his :
death a Chinese boy named Ah Ching. All j
tho vessels of the "Troop" fleet are known
as "hard ships" and the Howard D. ;
Troop; now on the Columbia River, is i
having considerable trouble in securing a
crew. The Mary A. Troop arrived at
Puget Sound the* latter part of last year j
ar.d she was not an hour in port before
the crew deserted. When she was ready I
to -.u'l another crew was procured after;
considerable trouble and among them was
Ah Ching. who shipped as cabin boy.
The unfortunate Mongol was the butt of
the captain and steward from the time the
ship passed out through the Straits of j
San Juan de Fuca. He -was beaten un- :
til his body was a mass of running sores,
he was compelled to eat rope yarn and
potato peelings and to lap up dirty water
from the deck. The crowning indignity,
from Ah Ching's point of view, was the
<-utting off e>f his pigtail. He then disap
peared overboard and is supposed to have
committed suicide.
\Vhen the Mary A. Troop arrived at
Buenos Ayres the crew went ashore and
made a statement to the British Consul.
He at once caused the arrest of Captain
Baker and Steward Pa - ! ;-ham and sent
them to England for trial. With thorn
went several of the sailors who are wit
nesses In the case.
Captain "Walley was placed in command
of the Mary A. Troop after Captain Ba
ker's arrest and he started from Buenos
Ayres fnr Hamburg in her. Had luck
seemed tn follow the ship after the death
of Ah <7hing and on March 26 the Troop
put into St. Thomas in distress. The cap
tain was sick, the enronometer broken
and the provisions had nearly all given
out.
Tho America Maru. which arrived from
thr* Orient last Saturday, brought the
news of the hard luck of the American
ship Sach*m. The vessel sailed from New
V'*rk for Shanghai with a cargo of kero
sene. When about half the voyage had
been covered Captain Schulz died from
Bright's diseast' of the kidneys and was
buried at sea. The mate then took charge
of the Sachem. In rhe Formosa channel
she ran into a terrific thunder storm. A
flash of lightning struck the ship and
the mate was knocked insensible. For
throe hours he lay as though dead and It
was only by the unremitting exertions
of the crew that his life was saved.
