CRUISE MARRED BY RAIN.
Yesterday's Storm Kept Many Yachts
at Their Moorings.
A second time the fates decreed against
a comblnc-d cruise of the yacht clubs. On j
Piimlay, April 23, the California and Cor- j
inthian fleets were to have met at Pre
sidio Shoal buoy and sailed in company;
alonp the city front. Despite half a gale!
from the southwest, the California^ were
at the rendezvous, but the Corinthians ,
did nut leave buron Cove.
On Saturday night more than thirty j
yachts of the San Francisco, Corinthian,
California and Encinal fleets lay at an- ;
chor in front of the San Francisco club- !
house, and everything pointed to a sue- j
cessful cruise on the morrow. But shortly |
alter 10 o'clock yesterday morning a
heavy rain set In. and though Commodore
T. 1.. Hill's flagship got under weigh
about 11 o'clock, and was followed by aev
eial yachts, all were soon lost to view
ln the obscuring rain. After sailing over
In the direction of the city front i he flag
ship Cygnus seiuared away for Raccoon
Btraits atul Mare Island. James Kitter
man's launch America started for Vallejo
about 11:20, but more than a |ozen yachts
remained at their moorings ail day.
Nearly all the yachts of the San Fran
cisco Club arc in commission now, except
G. T. K. White's Wiil o' the. Wisp, which
was found to be leaking considerably and
had to be beached, and W. S. i ; | . (low's
schooner La Paloma; which is still In
winter e.uartcrs in Sessions Basin, East
Oakland.

LUXOR WINS
THE STAKE AT
UNION PARK
Young America Goes
Down in Final.
The crowd which gathered at Union
Coursing Park yesterday to witness the
running down of the Class stake was
large The hares, however, were not so
tough as they had been on Saturday, and
the dogs all showed up in good form.
Luxor was victorious in the final run with
Young America. The course was short
and the decision was disputed by some,
but most were of the opinion that It was
correct. Connemara had been put out in
the fifth round by Luxor, after having
successively defeated Mercy May. Wait a
Little, Jessie Moore and Rollicking Airs.
The dog worked hard in its last two auc
â  --.â .*_
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, MAY 1, 1899.
cessful courses, and Luxor, having had
comparatively easy chases, was in much
better condition.
The first course of the afternoon, with
Lawrence and Theron in the slips, was of
particular interest, as botn the dogs have
been the winners of the last two stakes.
Theron sold a 5 to 2 favorite, but the
fraternity fell hard when Lawrence got
the flag. There were a number of short
ends during the afternoon, but no very
heavy odds were risked in any of them.
In the second round, Lawrence and
False Flatterer went to slips twice before
a decision was given. The last of the two
courses was Long and exciting. The match
between Victor Queen and Emm Pasha
was short.
Much dissatisfaction was expressed at
the decision in the course between Mercy
May and Interesting. The reason for
such dissension was the fact that Inter
etlng having become unsighted when
he left the slips had just recovered him
self when the kill was made and the de
cision given to Mercy May. The course
was so short that many of the spectators
believed the dogs had not even met and
expected no decision. However, one was
given and there was general disap
proval.
The reserve stake will be finished to-day.
The trains will leave on yesterday's
schedule time. The following are the re
sults In the class stake: - ; -':â _â "â

AMONG THE CRICKETERS.
British Sailors Tied Pacifies in the
First Match of the Season.
The first local cricket match of the
season was played on Saturday. April 22,
on the baseball ground in Golden Gate
Park between an eleven captained by C.
P. Coles of the Pacific club and a team of
apprentices from the British ship Crown
of India, King George, Coptic and Strath
don. The sailors were captained by the
Rev. H. W. L. O'Rorke, chaplain of the
Seamen's Institute of this city, . who
scored 19 runs for his side. The only other
batsman who scored double figures for
the sailors was Mr. Legg of the Coptic.
The innings closed for Cl runs. For the
j Pacifies C. P. Coles alone scored double i
figures, making IS runs. Shaw bowled
very successfully for the sailors, taking
six wickets. The Pacific innings closed
for 61 runs, and thus the match resulted
in a tie. C. Simmonds, formerly of the
Citrus Colony, Placer County, did good
service for the Pacifies, bowling three
men and catching three others.
The first match between clubs belong
ing to the California Cricket Association
will be played on Wednesday, May 3, in
Lake County between Burns Valley and
Lakeport teams, on the ground of the
former. The delegates of the Burns Val
ley club to the association have recently
been appointed. They are H. P. H. Hag
gitt and T. W. Beakbane. The Lake
County men are anxious that a team of
San Francisco cricketers should visit
them this season. They promise them
three matches and boundless hospitality.
In the last year of the century the Lake
County players hope to visit this city.
Burnell & Co., who last year gave a
bat, a pair of pads and a pair of batting
gloves to the association to be awarded
to the players making the best averages
in batting and bowling, have promised
three similar prizes this season.
The first match between city clubs will
be played on Sunday. May 7. at the Web
ster-street ground, Alameda, between the
Alameda and Pacific Cricket clubs. The
programme for the season Is in the hands
of the printer and will soon be issued.
It contains eighteen matches between the
Alameda, California and Pacific clubs for
the Brown pennant, to be presented at
the close of the season tp the club making
the best percentage.
A Musician's Suicide.
Albert Castagnetto, aged 20 years, a
musician, committed suicide by gas la
halation at 651 Vallejo street during the
early hours of Sunday morning. Despond
ency due to sickness is alleged as the
cause for his act. The deceased has a
brother connected with the undertaking
firm of Joe Godeau & Co.

Ariel Rowing Club Did Net Hold its

The proposed regatta of the Ariel Row

first, but was disqualified for dropping
part of the pie. W. Growney was ad

SOCIALISTS MAKE MERRY.
Grand Mayday Celebration by San
Francisco Section.
The San Francisco section of the So
cialist Labor party held a grand Mayday
ration at Union-square Hall last
evening. This is In ai cordance with the
annual custom of the party, which recog
nizes May l as the universal holiday of
the international labor movement.
An excellent programme had been pro
pared, and the large audience present
signified its approval of the various num
bers by prolonged and frequent applause,
The following was the programme which
was presented by the propaganda com
mittee and accepted by the city central
commit! of the organization:
Opening remarks by Chairman M. W. Wll
ktns; trombone solo, George Ames; "The Mar
seillaise." Herman Maennerchor; recitation;
George Aspden; song. Miss Verner; speech. Ar
thur Andre; speech. Grant Williams; recitation,
M. Noble; sons. Miss Aspden.
GUS STRAND CRAZY.
Arrested for Larceny, He Has an At-
tack of Delirium.
Gus Strand, a real estate agent, who
has several times been arrested for half
interest swindles, turned crazy in his cell
at the City Prison yesterday morning. He
imagined that the cell Boor was full of
trap doors and that people were trying
to kill him. it.- was sent to the insane
ward in I he Receiving Hospital.
Strand was arrested last Wednesday for
being concerned along with Kern Kenny
In a swindle whereby a Mrs. Meyer was
induced to part with"?7"> em the false rep
resentation that she was to get an In
terest in some business.
Some months ago while Strand was in
: the prison he attempted to hang himself
; in his cell; and made another attempt in
: the insane ward in the hospital. At that
I time he was suffering from the effects of
a prolonged debauch, and it is supposed
that he had been again drinking heavily
before being arrested.

Pierola's Illegal Closure.
LIMA. April 30 grams from all
parts of the republic censure the closure
of the electoral board. An article in a
semi-official organ, attributed to Pierola,
admits its illegality, but asserts that the
end iustilies the means. Things look dark,
as the legality of the electors appears
impossible.

ST, PATRICK'S
IMPRESSIVE
CEREMONIES
Large Class Receives
Confirmation.
THE ARCHBISHOP PRESENT
HE TALKS ELOQUENTLY TO THE
CHILDREN.
Father Dastler, Superior of the Re-
demptorists, Inaugurates a Mis-
sion for Women â Other
Interesting Services.
Decked anew in quiet, beautiful colors,
with myriad liphts shining from every
nook and corner, St. Patrick's Church, on
Mission street, was yesterday the scene ;
of three beautiful and impressive cere-
monies.
In the morning Father Dastler, superior
of the redemptorists, celebrated solemn
high mass and Inaugurated a mission for
women. Earlier in the day 300 of th*- boys
and girls of the parish received their first
| communion. At 4 o'clock in the after-
noon Most Rev. Archbishop Riordan ad-
ministered confirmation to another class
of 300 of the boys and girls who were
gathered to receive it. In the evening
Father McGeogh preached an eloquent
sermon.
The administration of confirmtlon in
th*- afternoon was a most beautiful cere-
mony. To a class of 300 of the brightest
boys and girls of the parish the Arch-
bishop spoke long and kindly of the duties
i they were about to take up as Catholic
citizens in the highest sense. He dwelt
â¢ upon the things they had been
taught In their Catholic training, |
â  and pointed out to them how I
, they might best adapt themselves to ]
j ful speech, brimming over with kindness I
; children with as rapt attention as by
. their parents and relatives, who crowded
the big church to the doorways.
All through the next month there will
be special devotions at St. Patrick's, and
the most eloquent Catholic fathers of the
city will be heard from time to time.
Father McKinnon of St. Jam* parish
celebrated mass in the evening at St.
Mary's.
CONDEMNED MOBS.
Rev. William Rader Delivers an Able
Sermon on the Recent Out-
rages in Georgia.
"Mobs and Government" was the sub-
ject of an Interesting address, delivered
by the Rev. William Rader last evening
before the members of the Third Congre-
gational Church. The sermon was based
, on the recent outrages committed in Geor- I
; gia by an infuriated moh and the speaker j
! condemned its lawless action. At the j
outset he said the mob crucified Christ,]
| reddened the streets of Paris and Ver- !
I sallies, blackened the name of Boston, I
j fired Philadelphia churches and disgraced I
| Chicago. It has lynched and burned and j
tortured both whites and blacks. In I
part he said: \
The burning of a negro in Georgia and th*> I
hanging of another on the evidence of the 1
first has brought the blush of shame to our
cheeks Such atrocities would make the Sultan |
of Turkey blush. The flames that consumed j
the wretch scorched the flag. For seven year:-) -
there has been on an average a negro burned i
for every year. The eight millions of negroes,
when educated. if that distant day ever comes, :
will make a rare conflict If they remain in j
i their Ignorance there will be a race war. |
Block Justice in the courts and you make a I
mob on the streets. The atrocity of the crimes I
i Incites the passions of men, which, influenced I
: by race hatred, develon the lynching mania.
Such fiends should be punished, not by lawless I
mobs but by lawful and speedy methods, Mobs I
: are anarchists. They are un-American. They I
sow seeds of lawlessness among the vicious j
' classes. The tendency toward lawlessness in
our country is seen in the fact that a San ]
; Francisco evening paper defends the atrocities
I of the South in a lengthy editorial. That is a ;
dangerous sign of the times.
! The remedy lies In a profounder respect for I
! law, as a principle of righteousness and as a i
method of Justice. Whenever an infuriated
populace takes the laws of a community into j
its own hands democracy Is defeated and the i
flag insulted. While we are expressing our hor-
ror at Spanish atrocities and respecting the 11- j
lustrious name of Dewey, let us remember that
self-government at home ls the surest guaran- !
tee to the world of our ability to govern abroad
and that to the extent of our Inability to place I
1 within the reach of the greatest wretch the j
instruments of .lustier- we shall become the j
I lauphing stock of the nations of the earth.

PROBABLY THE
LAST HEARD OF
FABIOLA DAY
Opposition Does Not
Prove Acceptable.
THE LADIES ARE BLAMELESS
WORTHY CRUSADE AGAINST
VILE SALOONb.
Dens That Cost the City Very Many
Times the Amount Paid Into
the Treasury for
Licenses.
Oakland Offlce Pan Francisco Call, S
908 Broadway, April 29.
The action of the License Committee In
mending to the Council that no
liquor licenses be granted to houses where
minora have been served or crimes com
mltted; or which are known to he the re- I
sort of thieves and other disreputable
characters, is eminently proper, and is to !
be encouraged and commended. When
ever such a course has been suggested in ;
the past it has always been met by the
practice of applying for a license under
a new name, as though a dandelion would ,
smell like a rose because called one. The
habitues and environments of a saloon
determine Its character, and these are not
changed because the ex-proprietor be
comes the bartender and the nominal pro
prietor is never or seldom seen.
There tire in Oakland fully fifty dead
falls which should be closed and which !
are costing the clty many times the
amount of the license they put in the
treasury. They harbor the very worst
types of men and women; and give the
â¢ more trouble than a score of sa
foons decently conducted. Occasionally a j
murder, or attempted murder*, or crimes
equally henlous; are committed, and tho
city and county are put to great expense
in "trying the cases, which would never
have "come up but for these dens. The
abolishing of such places would be a wise
economy. ____________
Mayor Snow has In his possession a ''.
statement from two lads now awaiting
trial for burglary, in which they tell him
*f the particular saloon where their crime
was hatched, and where they were served
with liquor, although both are minors.
Any man who would vote to continue
a license to such a house is unworthy of
a seat in the City Council or anywhere
else outside the pillory of public indigna
tion and contempt.
The Supervisors will meet to-morow,
nnd if they do what is requested of them
they will revoke the license of the notori
ous Thalia saloon on the San leandro
road. This place was, a few days ago,
the scene of a most diabolical orgy. The
details have been suppressed, but enough |
is known to justify very peremptory ac- i
tion. This is not the first time that this i
resort has been a scene of bloodshed;
but decency demands that the affair of
last Sunday shall be the last. ;
I have no patience with that large class '
of people who live by their wits and who j
are forever opposing any attempt to pro
mote decency and order on the ground
that it Is a movement to make Oakland a
"blue-law" town. Such a suggestion is I
as ridiculous as it is insincere. Because
an effort be made to close a number of
notorious haunts of vice, there is no sense
in declaring that prohibition Is the end in ,
view; because vile keno dens are driven
out to Emeryville is no argument that
every place of legitimate amusement is
being turned into ii prayer-mi
house; because vagrants, hoodlums anil
tinhorn gamblers are compelled to move
on or out is surely no excuse for saving
that no one can live in peace in this city. i
Vet this is the view taken by those who i
would subvert every attempt to suppress
vir.> nd to maintain law and to prevent '
high school students and others from that
class being exposed to temptations which
they have nut experience enough to
understand.

Practical variation of an old adage. He
has evidently heard somewhere that there
is a torrid place which is paved with good
intentions. He makes the application to
Fablola's Derby day and very aptly.
Many well-meaning folk have inadver
tently created the impression that the
ladies who conduct that magnificent insti
tution have locked hands with the ele
ments of viciousness and have entered
into a contract to boom and extend the
manifest evils of the racetrack. This is
absurd, but not an altogether unreason
able deduction. The ladles of Fabiola
have but one object in the premisesâ the
welfare of Fabiola, and If some other
source of benefit can be devised the
ladies will most willingly accept It. Dr.
Sunderland says that it is all very well
for the Christians to stand up in prayer
meeting and denounce Fabiola dayâ such
efforts are easy, but offer no remedy. He
proposes that 'all unite in raising a sum
annually equal to the amount received
from Fabiola day and then the Purpose of
such protest is accomplished. This is
right. Oakland Is guilty of too much re
solving and subsequent inactivity. * â¢ ,
There Is something remarkably logical
in Dr. Sunderland's suggestion, and it will
no donbt appeal to the i lies of t-abiota.
Argument on such a question is vain.
Conviction can only be arrived at from a
knowledge of practical example. There
a?e many among the budding manhood
and womanhood of our society who un
doubtedly take advantage of Fabiola day
to obtain their first experience of the
racetrack. If it could be proved that

The manner in which a grand jury can
be persuaded to take up an alleged griev
ance, which is based on purely personal
motives, is rlalnly shown in the case of
ex-Coroner Evers. Some years ago a
grand jury declared that Mr. Kvers had
overcharged the county about $120*3 dur
ing his term of oflice. It now appears
that Coroners for years before Mr. Evers
was elected, the Coroner that succeeded
him and the present Coroner have made
exactly the same charges. Yet Evers
was put to heavy expense to defend him
self from these "suits, while the charges
i*re still ing made and successive grand
iuries have not thought lit to make any
more indictments. Why Evers should
have been singled out would no doubt
make a good story.
Thursday night a cultured, though
very small audience at the Macdonough,
was quietly rebuked by the artist Rosen
thal. During the rendition of a number
was heard the squeaking of a pair of boots
in the balcony, where are supposed to be
seated the genuine music lovers. The
pianist refused to play in competition to
boots. He stopped abruptly. The squeaks
kept on, then subsided. The pianist re
sumed, and Oakland people were made
aware of the fact that if they could not
support an artist for one night at the
Macdonough, neither could they annoy
him. When the Macdonuogh has added a
dispel for people who must cough
and a conversation room for people who
must talk after coming to the theater,
then it wil indeed be a model playhouse.
It was quite too bad that this modern
Athens should have suffered such a re
buke on account of the carelessness of
one "music lover."
What ls the value of a Jury? This ques
tion is brought up by the remarkable ex
hibition of injustice meted out in Justice
Quinn's court last Thursday. There were
six jurors and supposedly six mini and
intellects. There might have been two.
but. there were no more, or else, as the
Court said,* there was corruption and
crookedness. When the two referred to
have, been determined the other four are
at liberty to attempt to receive $10>)
apiece by the grace of one Morehouse of
the late unlamentcd Legislature.
Mayor Snow has a kodak with which
he Intends to secure absolute proof of
the existence of nuisances and of neces
sary repairs. If nuisances be held to in
clude human beings, who are apparently
sworn to prevent the progress of this
city, the Mayor's term of office should
be at least ten years. In no less time
can he complete the collection.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, MAY 1,1899.
NO NEED TO
PAY INTEREST
FOR SALARIES
Treasurer Gilpin Has
a Scheme.
WORKED WELL YEARS AGO
LAKE MERRITT FUND MAY NOT
BE SACRED.
Work Done After June May Have to
Paid for From the Income for
the Next Fiscal
Year.
Oakland Offlce San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, April 30.
"If I were permitted to act I could fix
matters in flve minutes so that no em
ploye of the city would have to pay 8
per cent interest, or any interest, to any
bank on his salary Warrants for the next
few months," said Treasurer Gilpin.
The prospect of every employe of the
city of Oakland having to pay 8 per cent
interest to some bank for the purpose
of drawing salary on their warrants, un
til some money is received after next
July, is causing considerable worry at the
City Hall. Mr. Gilpin, who has handled
the city cash for ten years, says he can
adjust matters if permitted to do so, al
though his methods might be considered
to be not in harmony with the law. The
method, however, was in force for many
years, and during that time the banks
cashed warrants at their face value, with
out any interest.
About three years ago the City Council
passed an order that no city money was
to be put on deposit in the local banks,
nut that the city, not having a fire-proof
vault of its own, should rent a deposit
box in one of the banks and hoard its
money in it. Since that time no money
has been on deposit in banks, and conse
quently the banks are under no obliga
tion to the city to cash warrants without
interest. There is now in the city's de
posit box a sum of money sufficiently
large to enable all salary warrants to be
paid at tin face value, If the Treasurer
be permitted to carry into effect his lit
tle scheme, which is to take the money
from the box and put it on deposit. The
banks then, having the use of the money,
would make It earn the interest which
they would otherwise charge the em
ployes.
The county of Alameda has done for
years past just what Mr. Gilpin wants to
do, and thing has been said. In the
case of the county there Is not the excuse
of there being no adequate treasury in
which to keep it, and yet here is never
a time when there is not from a quarter
to a half million dollars of the county's
funds In the local hanks.
There is also some doubt as to whether
the $48,000 now held to pay for the work
on Lake Merritt can be retained for that :
purpose after June 30, if the Council shall
undertake to transfer it to the salary
fund, and the law permits. After June
30 it is calculated that at least $30,
of this fund will not have been earned,
and consequently the work done after
that date will ii*- chargeable to the in
come of the city for the next fiscal year. }
There are differences of opinion regarding >
this trasfer of funds, but everybody seems
willing to strain a point if all the em
ployes can be saved from having to pay
8 per cent interest on their warrants,
especially now that after May 1 nearly
all such warrants have already been re
duced 25 per cent by ordinance.

UNIVERSITY OF
CALIFORNIA NEWS
BERKELEY, April 30.â The present
academic year of the University of Cali
fornia will i"- brought to a close on
Wednesdty, May 17. From that date the
vacation will last continuously for three
months, college convening again about
the middle of August.
All recitations for the term stopped last
Thursday. The final examinations for
the year, the examination constituting
the basis for awarding the bachelor's de
gree, commenced yesterday, They will
continue for two weeks, with the excep
tion Of to-morrow, being a legal holiday.
On Saturday, May 13, the graduating class
will hold its class day exercises upon the
campus. The exercises, as in previous
years, will be in the nature of a grand
pageant, each graduate appearing in fan
tastic costume as part of a spectacular
performance, intended as the solemn ob
sequies of tho parting class. Arrange
ments have been made to hold the rites
in the natural amphitheater on the upper
campus, behind the chemistry building.
The big circus which was to have been
laid last Thursday afternoon has been set
for to-morrow at the same time. ISO
changes have been made in the pro
gramme, and it will bo given as an
nounced previously.
The members of Phi Beta Kappa, the
honorary scholarship fraternity for which
the university was recently granted a
charter arc planning to hold a banquet
in San Francisco, Saturday evening, May
13. All the fraternity alumni on the coast
are expected to be present. As a part of
the literary exercises of the occasion Pro
fessor Charles Mills Gayley of the Eng
lish department has accepted the invita
tion to read a poem.
SLASHED WITH A RAZOR.
A Painful Handshake With a
Stranger.
OAKLAND, April 30.â Denne, a
house-mover, residing at 866 Lydia street,
was treated at the Receiving; Hospital
shortly after 1 o'clock this morning for a
severely cut right wrist. The arteries
and tendons were severed and the young
man would have bled to death had
a friend not met him <n time and caused
him to be taken to the hospital.
Denne's Injury was Inflicted with a
sharp razor in the hands of some un
known person. He had been drinking
quite freely and at the corner of Seventh
and Washington streets a stranger en
tered into conversation with him. This
was at about 1 o'clock. Denne says the
fellow shook hands with him then left
him. and soon after he discovered he was
bleeding at the wrist. The injury dazed
him and he knew not what to do until
his friend approached him and called
for the patrol. Drs. Stratton and Roue
and Steward Borchart worked on Denne
several hours and with considerable diffi
culty saved his life. The case is surround
ed with considerable mystery.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
With the exception of Southern California and
Arizona the weather is generally cloudy over
the Pacific slope. Light rain has fallen from
the Tehachapi and Central Nevada northward
to the British Possessions.
The pressure la rising over the northwestern
portion of the country and falling elsewhere
over the Paclflc slope. â¢ .' ....
The temperature has risen over Arizona and ,
the eastern portion of Southern California and
fallen in the remaining districts. - - ,-
Conditions are favorable for fair weather In
California Monday, except in the mountains of
the southeast portion, where it will be stormy.
There will be a general fall in temperature,
especially over Arizona and the southeastern
portion of California.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, May 1. 1599:
Northern Californiaâ Monday, except
showers in the mountains of the southeast
portion; cooler; brisk to high northwest wind.
Southern Californiaâ cloudy Monday,
with showers in the mountains; cooler in north
portion; brisk northerly wind.
Nevadaâ Cloudy Monday, probably with occa
sional rain or snow; cooler.
Cloudy, with showers, cooler Monday.
Arizonaâ Partly cloudy In south portion, rain
or snow in north portion; cooler.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Monday;
brisk to high northwest wind.
G. H. WILLSON,

NOTICE TO MARINERS. I
A branch of the United States Hydrographlc
Offlce, located ln the Merchants' Exchange, ls
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
offlce. where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball en the tower of the new Ferry
building at the foot of Market street 1.- hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory, Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any, Is published
ln the morning papers the following day.
C. G. CALKINS,
Lieutenant, U. S. N., in charge.

NOTE â In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day ln the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign â precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low waters.

THE OLYMPIC
TRY-OUT WAS A
GREAT SUCCESS
Wind and Rain No
Bar to Cycling.
PREPARING FOR RELAY RACE
ONLY FOUR TEAMS WILL CON
TEST THIS YEAR.
Thirty-Two Riders Go Over tho
Courses in the Mud Across
the Bayâ The Re
sults.
The rain of yesterday did not prevent
the hardy road riders of the Olympic
and Bay City clubs from holding their I
last try-outs preliminary to the great an- ;
nual relay race.
The Olympics rode fourteen men over 1
the 10-mile triangle course, starting from
a point near San Leandro, while the Bay
Cltys put eighteen of their best and speed
iest over the five-mile course from San
Leandro to Haywards.
The rain, which commenced falling just !
as the club men reached San Leandro,
drenched the enthusiastic riders, their j
thin racing suits doing admirable service
at bathing apparel. But with the relay
race only two weeks in the future and
with the absolute necessity of picking out
men for the teams, mud and rain did not
daunt the hardy roadsters.
C. S. Myrick officiated as starter for
the Olympic race, the men starting two \
minutes apart and riding unpaced. The
summary Is as follows:
riace. Contestant. j Time, i
II R. O. Kragness ' 25:50

3 E. A. B**zi.j 29:17:01
4 E. F. Kuss 29:42:02
5 Kd. McNess 29:45
6 George Fuller 30:01

S George Kroetz 30:26:02

starters in their race and all finished,
though Robert Black sustained a fall.

NOTICE is hereby given by order of the Board
of Directors of the OCEANIC STEAMSHIP
COMPANY that a meeting of the stockholders
of said Company has been called by said
Board, to be held on FRIDAY, the second day
of June, A. 1). ISOO, at eleven o'clock in the
forenoon of said day. at the principal place of i :
business of said Company, at the building
where the said Board of Directors usually
meets, namely, at the office of said Company,
number 327 Market st., In the City and County
of San Francisco, State of California: that
the object of said meeting Is to consider and |
act upon the proposition that said Company j
create a bonded indebtedness of two million ;
five hundred thousand dollars ($2,500,000 00), In '
United States gold coin, for the purpose of
raising money to complete the construction
of its steamships and their equipment, for
use in the business of this corporation, and
to purchase and pay for any other property
within the purposes of this Company: and to
secure the bonded Indebtedness so proposed
to be created by a mortgage upon Its steam
and sailing ships and all other property of
said Company now owned or hereafter to be
acquired by said Company.
By order of the Board of Directors of the
Oceanic Steamship Company.
[Corporate Seal.] E. H. SHELDON.

â  PROPOSALS. j
PROPOSALS
To Print, Publish and Distribute the De-
linquent Tax List for IS9S.
OFFICE OF THE CLERK OF THE
Board of Supervisors of the City and
County of San Francisco, April 25, 1899. ;
In accordance with Resolution No. 2026
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Supervis-
ors, SEALED PROPOSALS will be re-
ceived in open session of the Board of Su-
pervisors on MONDAY AFTERNOON,
May 8, 1899. from 2:30 to 3 o'clock, for the
printing, publishing and distributing of
the delinquent tax list for the year 1898.
to contain the names of the persons and
a description of the property delinquent
and the amount of taxes and costs due
opposite each name, and a notice unless
the taxes delinquent, together with the
costs and percentage, are paid, the prop-
erty upon which said taxes are a lien
will be sold at public auction. Such
publication- to be made and thoroughly
distributed on a business day, at least
once in each week, .for three successive
weeks, in a pamphlet fern as a supple-
ment to a daily newspaper of general cir-
culation, published in the city and
county of San Francisco, in the

WITTMAN AND
MORAN MEET AS
STRANGERS
Trouble Between the
Two Captains.
WERE FRIENDS FOR YEARS
COULD NOT STAND HIS SEVERE
CRITICISM.
The Property Clerk Demands of
Chief Lees to Prefer Charges
Against "Handsome
George."
Captain Wittman of the Central station
and Captain Moran, the Property Clerk
of the Police Department, are no longer
friends. In a moment of anger the
Central station captain referred in a dis
respectful manner to Moran, and as the
result they do not speak as they pass by.
The incident which brought about the
strained relations which now exist be
tween the two police officials occurred in
Moran's offlce. Wittman. accompanied by
several of his men, at the conclusion of
the hearing of the Junction proceedings
instituted by the Pacific Coast Jo* key
Club against Chief I_*-*-s. visited the Prop
erty Clerk's otlice and instructed Moran
to have the evidence in the case ready by
the following morning. While Wittman
was dilating on the necessity of having
the evidence ready, Moran stood among
the group of policemen nodding assent to
what the Central station captain was say
ing. Suddenly stopping in the rriiast of
his remarKs Wittman savagely turned on
Moran nnd accused him of humorously
treating him.
"This is no joke." he hotly declared, his
face Hushed with anger. "You can
shake your head; hut it don't go with
me."
Moran was so astonished at the declara
tion of Wittman that ho could not think
nf a reply. Trembling with suppressed
anger Moran walked out of his office,
leaving Wittmann to finish his remarks
about the evidence in the case to his
squad of officers.
Th. following day the two captains met
in Chief Lees' office. Still smarting under
th*- humiliation of being degraded by a
man of the same rank, Moran dramatic
ally pointed his finger at Wittman, and
addressing Chief Lees, said:
â¢â I d< mand of you to prefer charges of
disrespect against Captain Wittman. In
the presence of his men he not only de
graded mo. but by Innuendo accused mo
of trying to suppress the evidence in the
Injunction proceedings Instituted by the
Pacific Coast Jcol-ey * 'Ill."
Still pointing his finger at the Central
station captain, who had become speech
less by the suddenness of the attack,
Moran declared that even the policemen
who had heard the burning words of con
demnation of their superior. officer subse
quently came to him and extended their
svmpa'thv.
"They wanted to apologize for this
man's conduct." remarked Moran. "as
they fully realized that he had made an
unwarranted and brutal attack on me."
By this time Wittman had recovered
his powor of speech, and, facing Moran,
demanded to know the names of the po
licemen who wanted to sympathize with
him.
"That is mv business." hotly retorted
Moran. "You will have to get their names
from them."
"You're only making a bluff." angrily
remarked Wittman. "You ought, to con
fine yourself to the facts."
"1 have a witness, a responsible person,
who is hero an.l will corroborate what I
say." testily answered Moran.
Turning to Chief T.ees he again de
manded that he prefer charges against
the Central station captain. While the
head of the department was deprecating
the occurrence Moran turned to Witt
man and declared that he did not want
to know him in the future.
"We moot as captains of police," he re
marked, "but as friends, never."
"I have been in this department for over
twenty years. and not once have I been
before the Police Commissioners, charged
with misconduct. T am sorry to say that
you cannot boast of the same record."
Moran was about to refer to the bribery
charges-- which were brought against
"Witt! and which were dismissed by
the Police Commissioners, when Chief
Lees called a halt. Both men then left
Lees' offlce ami went to heir respective
departments. Xow they meet as strangers
âone revengeful nd the other awaiting
an opportunity to strike back.
SOUTHERN OUTRAGES.
Bey. T. Brown of the A. M. E. Zion
Church Proclaims Against
Them.
Rev. T. Brown, pastor of Starr Kin?
African Methodist Episcopal Zlon Church,
delivered an address last night on the
recent, inhuman lynching tragedies com
mitted in Georgia, Louisiana, Texas and
elsewhere in the South. He Inveighed bit
terly against the brutal outrages, which
he characterized as the most awful and
. horrible in the most civilized age known
In the world's history.
"The Egyptian bondage of the Jews for
43*1 years." the reverend speaker said In
the course of his remarks, "cannot be
compared with the barbarity going on in
our own Christian land. The murder of
Bam Hose by burning him to a stake for
a supposed crime is a disgrace to the
civilization of America. The murder of
the aged preacher Strickland by lynch
ing and the cutting up of the bod!' as
souvenirs is the essence of barbarism. It
appears that Christ and the colored man
nre the only two beings who have not the
friendship of the world. It gives rise to
the question, 'Shall the United States con
tinue to be the colored man's home or
shall he look for another?'
"Give the colored man protection or
give him death. No people have done
more to build up the great commonwealth
than the colored Americans. The negroes
fought loyally for the. old flag, but the
old flag cannot protect them. In four
great national wars the colored man took
an active part. He has never yet proved
a traitor, yet he is murdered and the
general Government looks calmly on. Tie
can only protect himself by racial unity.
"I hope the time is not far distant "â¢ en
the tree of peace shall spread its broad
branches over tiie land: that there will
Tie no more lynching and nil men will he
living in perfect national peace every
where."

at f<2o Sacramento street to-day at 10:30

ham H. Roberts' "Defense of Polygamy,"
a work that has attracted much interest
throughout the religious world. The re
view will be made solely on a religious
basis, not -scientific, the moral ef
fect rather thai the causes and ultimate
results of polygamy constituting the lec
turer's theme.

DEATH STAYED
THE ARM OF
THE SUICIDE
Remarkable End of
H. L. Kirchoff.
HIS REMAINS TO SCIENCE
DIED FROM ASTHMA WITH
POISON AT HIS SIDE.
A Letter to the Coroner Shows That
He Had Intended to
Take the Fatal
Draught.
. -
The taking off of H. If. Kirchoff ln a
id-street lodging house yesterday
afternoon brought to light one of the most
peculair cases the Coroner's office has
been called upon to handle for a . long
time. Everything goes to show that the
man, driven to desperation by poverty
and sickness, had intended taking his
own life. At the last moment death
stayed the suicide's arm, and the man
for whom life held nothing more was
taken away.
When the landlady went to Kirchoff'p
room yesterday morning she met no re
sponse to repeated knocks. Thinking
that her lodger, whom she knew to be in
poor health, had mercifully fallen into a
refreshing sleep, she went away. In the
afternoon she called again, but could get
no answer. The door was finally forced
open and Kirchoff was found lying still
and cold upon the bed. On a table at his
head was a bottle of laudanum, and be
si*!. it the following remarkable letter:
Dear â oner: In case I should he found
dead blame no one hut myself. I am suffering
with an incurable sickness known as asthma-
There is no cure for it and the suffering is un
bearable. My money Is gone anil death will
be welcome. I have no friends or relatives
(.live my hody to the doctors. Let them cut
it up. They can learn something from me. I
have been ruptured for forty-two years and
have a skin that no other man has.
H. L. KIRCHOFF.
Another letter was drawn up in the
form of a will. In it he bequeathed all
his personal belongings a lit
tle furniture and some beds and bed
clothing to Mrs. E. Badger of 74.A Natoma
street for attending him. both while he
was ill at her former residence, 115 Second
street, ami since that time. He also
stated that I;.- owed nothing, either to the
landlady or nny one else. lb- was 72
years of age. and according to his own
statement had no relatives living.
BAINTALL WAS LIGHT.
San Francisco Is Given a Slight Wet-
ting With Plenty of Wind.
Rain began falling in San Francisco at
4 a. m. yesterday and continued intermit
tently until shortly after noon, but the
j precipitation was light. The storm start
ed on the coast of Washington and
' worked its way south along the coast as
â¢ far as Tehachapi, extending also into the
interior, with a light fall of snow in the
foothills. The wind blew with consider
able violence all day, reaching its great
est velocity at about 10 a. m., when the
gauge on the top of the Mills building
registered thirty-four miles an hour. At
5 p. m. it was blowing twenty-four miles
an hour. On Mount Tamalpais the wind
blew fifty miles an hour. Forecaster Mc
: Adie says the storm is over for the pres-
I ent and there will be no rain to-day, al
though the indications are that the wind
will blow and the weather will continue
cool.
Friends of the Indian.
The San Francisco Protestant Indian
Association will meet at 717 Post street
to-morrow afternoon at 3 o'clock. All
friends interested in the spiritual and
moral uplifting of the Indian are earnest
ly and cordially invited to be present.

GROSSER OF
THE RUBICON
HAS ARRIVED
General Harrison G.
Otis on Politics.
AFFAIRS IN THE PHILIPPINES
â 
WILL LEAVE FOR LOS AN
GELES IMMEDIATELY.
The General Thinks the War in
the Far East Will Be Ended
in a Very Few
Weeks.
The modern Caesar has arrived. The
valiant brigadier from the Southland,
whose cablegram from Manila announc
ing that he had "crossed the Rubicon"
thrilled the hearts and sent the blood
leaping with Joyous bounds through the
veins of his vassals in Los Angeles is
here, having reached this port on the
transport Sherman en route to his home,
where 3 reception befitting his deeds of
daring on the bloody battlefields of the
Far East awaits him.
General Harrison Gray Otis' achieve
ments in the Philippine campaign are
current history, but the deed which is
destined to send his name echoing down
the corridors of time is his daring emu
lation of the Roman conqueror. With a
single stroke of the pen he put himself on
a plane far above all other military heroes
of the epoch and took his place on the
pinnacle of fame along with the greatest
heroes of ancient and modern times.
Lawton, Mac Arthur, Wheaton and the
namesake of this nineteenth century
Caesar may win battles, as they are doing
daily and batter the half-saVage Tagallos
into submission to Uncle Sam, but their
victories pale in comparison with the
feat of Harrison Gray Otis of Los Ange
les. They are not the only generals who
have the abality to win battles, but who
save he could cross the Rubicon without
getting wet.
But what has. induced this warrior of
Los Angeles to leave the scene of war
where glory is to be bought at the can
non's mouth? Why return to the land or
peace and orange blossoms? The whisper
comes from the South that politics is the
moving cause; that the newly-created
Caesar must leave the hard field of noisy
battle for the soft endeavors of politics.
"I have returned because my business
and civil interests demanded it." said
General Otis yesterday afternoon as he
stamped up and down the reading room
of the Occidental Hotel. "J have not
given politics any thought, and. if I had,
what of it?. You ask me if 1 intend going
as a delegate at large to the next Na
tional Republican convention? It is ab
surd to figure on anything so far in ad
vance. I do not wish to say anything
more on that matter. If you wish to
know anything about the Philippines, I
will most willingly edify you. I think
that the war in the Philippine Islands is
about ended. The natives have neither
supplies nor ammunition. They are with
out discipline, order or leaders, and they
can not last more than a few weeks at
the most. The war is practically over."
The general showed great pertuhatlon
of mind when the subject of politics was |
again approached, and he would have no :
more of it. _________________
DEWEY DAY FEATURES AT
CATHOLIC LADIES' FAIR
PATRIOTIC SONGS AND RECITA
TIONS WILL BE GIVEN.
The Voting Contest for the Most Pop
ular Policeman or Fireman
Gaining in Interest.
Dewey day will be appropriately cele
brated at the Catholic Ladles' Aid So
ciety's bazaar, a special programme in
cluding many pleasing features having
been arranged for the occasion. .To-night
will be "Native Sons' and Daughters'
night," and Dennis Foley's orchestra of
twelve pieces will render patriotic music
gratuitously; Mr. Gildea will sing origi
nal compositions on Admiral -Dewey; Mr.
Morrisey will â¢ recite, and Mrs. Fitzsim
mons will render patriotic selections on
the harp. The decorations for the occa
sion are donated by Mr. Sleicher.
The voting contest for the most popu
lar policeman or fireman will be confined
during the remaining week of the fair,
in accordance with the original plan, to
the three standing at the head of the list
in each department. A total of 782 votes
have been cast, and of these Sergeant J.
B. Martin has received 133, Sergeant T.
Shea 127, Sergeant M. .1. Conboy 119. Chief
of the Fire Department D. T. Sullivan
123. Assistant Engineer P. H. Shaugh
nessy 107 and Assistant Engineer M. J.
Dolan 93. One of the six named will se
cure the coveted prize. Sergeant Martin
at present heads the list, but the friends
of each of the other five are confident
that their candidate will have the major
ity of votes when the polls close on Sat
urday night. The fair is gaining in pop
ularity daily, and it is almost certain to
prove the financial success that was ex
pected for it. '*.-"â¢.â¢
A MISSION PANHANDLE.
Superintendent McLaren's Scheme to
Make a Boulevard of Dolores
Street.
Park Superintendent McLaren has hit
upon a plan, devised during the rainy
days when the park was deserted, as
yesterday, that will loubtless please the
people of the Mission and be a great ad
dition to the present scheme to extend the
panhandle to Van Ness avenue. Mr. Mc-
Laren is of the opinion that Dolores
Street, which is wide and for a long way
out into the Mission is free from car
tracks, should be made into a boulevard
and trees set along the curb as far out as
Twenty-sixth street. Then Buchanan
street at the junction of that thorough
fare and Dolores should be widened also
into a boulevard to the proposed exten
sion of the panhandle. This would give
the people of the Mission a panhandle to
the park.
The park was deserted yesterday. It
was free day for the children at the
Chutes, as it will be to-day, and hundreds
of youngsters enjoyed themselves. The
baby monkey will be named to-day and
the winner of this novel contest will take
home either a bicycle or sewing machine.
At Sutro's Baths the Japanese troupe
of acrobats and expert swimmers enter
tained the guests. Following are the re
sults of the aquatic contests: 50-yard
dash, for novices, won by R. Bedford,
J. O'Brien second; 50-yard dash, for ju
veniles, won by C, Donovan. R. Bedford
second; 170-yard dash, for amateurs, won
by W. Hurley, R. Dahl second: high div
ing, won by O. Schulte; trapeze diving,
won by Pi 1 Guerin, O. Bey f us second.
